Preface 
The present thesis was carried out from 23rd of January 2017 to 23rd of June 2017 at the 
Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark and accounts for 30 
ECTS credits. It was carried out under the supervision of Professor Svend Svendsen. 
I would like to thank my supervisor, Professor Svend Svendsen, for his guidance and 
assistance regarding the investigation procedure carried for this thesis. His ideas inspired me 
and motivated me to accomplish this project.

Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy

requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020

Abstract 
In this thesis, an investigation procedure was performed in order to develop an office 
building system, based on the integrated design approach of space of solutions and the 
requirements of nZEB, as they are defined in the Building class of 2020 in the 2015 Danish 
Building Regulations and the EN 15251 standards. Several design parameters were varied and 
their influence in fulfilling the design requirements was evaluated, in order to provide flexibility 
regarding the needs of different clients. The investigation was performed by using the 
commercial software of IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (IDA ICE) version 4.7.1 by EQUA. 
The office building system was modeled as building modules of 3 m façade length, based 
on the space of solution approach. Office building modules of different building width and 
different number of workplaces close to both facades were investigated. The investigation 
process was initially focused on evaluating and optimizing the minimum Daylight Factor (DF) 
and the primary energy use for artificial lighting, in relation to the glazing area and the 
properties of the window elements on the façade. Several window variations of different glazing 
area, different window type and different glazing properties were implemented and evaluated 
in the building modules of different width. In order to minimize the energy requirements for 
artificial lighting, different solutions were investigated. A lighting control strategy based on the 
dimming operation of the lighting system was implemented, the use of a lower illuminance set 
point value for the operation of the lighting system was evaluated and finally, the thickness of 
the façade element was minimized. All the three different solutions resulted into a significantly 
low share of the overall primary energy use of 25 kWh/m2, related to the energy requirements 
for artificial lighting. However, the primary energy use was increasing as the width of the 
building module was higher, and the glazing area of the window elements was reducing. 
To optimize the ventilation system, the Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) approach 
based on CO2 concentration was implemented in the different modules. Regarding the 
evaluation and the optimization of the energy requirements for heating and cooling, different 
solutions were investigated, as a supplement to the main mechanical systems for space heating 
and cooling, based on District Heating (DH) and electricity respectively. The first solution was 
the use of external blinds, operating dynamically according to the solar irradiation level. The 
second solution was the use of glazing units with solar control coating.  
According to the results, the energy requirements for cooling was the dominant share of the 
overall primary energy use in most of the different examined variations. The use of the external 
blinds resulted into higher primary energy use for lighting and heating comparing to the use of 
the glazing units with solar control coating, which resulted into significantly higher primary 
energy use for cooling. The results indicated that according to the selected electrical based 
cooling system, the use of the external blinds was the recommended solution. However, if a 
cooling system based on Renewable Energy Sources (RES) was used to provide the energy 
requirements for cooling without cost, then the solution of glazing units with solar control 
coating would be the optimal solution, taking also into account the lower initial an operation 
cost and the fact that it was a robust solution. In addition, it was noticed that in the building 
modules of lower number of workplaces in a row close to the facades, the overall primary 
energy use could be further reduced, due to the lower acceptable glazing area based on the 
minimum DF, in comparison with the building modules of higher number of workplaces.  
Furthermore, the effect of the occupancy level on the primary energy use of different 
building modules was investigated. According to the results, when the occupancy level was 
reduced from 100% to 75% or 50%, the primary energy use for cooling and ventilation was 
significantly reduced, while there was an increase in the energy requirements for heating. 
Although, the overall primary energy use was significantly reduced in all the different 
variations examined. 
The indoor climate results, due to the continues operation of the heating and cooling systems 
during the year combined with the use of Proportional-Integrated (PI) controllers, were 
fulfilling the thermal comfort requirements, as they were defined in EN 15251 standards.

Chapter 1: Introduction 
1.1. Background – Near Zero Energy Buildings 
In order to preserve a sustainable fossil-free energy future and reduce the greenhouse gas 
emissions along with their negative effects in the climate change, the reduction of the energy 
use and the implementation of sustainable sources in the energy mix is quite essential. 
According to the 2010/31/EU directive on the energy performance of buildings (EPBD) [1], the 
building sector is responsible for the 40% of the total energy use among the countries members 
of the European Union. Therefore, the decrease of the energy use of the building sector and the 
implementation of renewable sources to produce the required energy, are important measures 
that can reduce the overall energy dependency and the greenhouse gas emissions in the EU [1]. 
In this direction, the European Parliament in its resolution of 3 February 2009 on the Second 
Strategic Energy Review, set the energy efficient target of 20% energy use reduction in the 
building sector, national binding targets for CO2 emissions reduction and a binding target for 
20% energy production from sustainable sources regarding the total energy production. All 
these targets were set to be accomplished by 2020[1]. 
Denmark is one of the leading countries regarding the establishing of the green growth 
economy and a fossil-free future, by setting additional energy targets for sustainable 
development. More specifically, the Danish Government in the report “Energy Strategy 2050 
– from coal, oil and gas to green energy” on February 2011 [2], set a target of 100% energy 
production from sustainable sources by 2050. There are also intermediate targets of 35% 
greenhouse gas emission reduction by 2020 compared to 1990 and the electricity and heat 
supply to be covered by renewable energy by 2035 [2]. Finally, in the building sector, it is 
proposed that the new buildings should be designed to have high energy efficiency achieved 
with the most modern and cost effective technologies and the designers to be in integration with 
the  market sector to obtain the optimal results regarding the energy efficiency [2].  
Article 9 of the EPBD directive [1], introduces the term of Nearly zero-energy buildings 
(nZEB) to define a high energy efficient building. The nearly zero or quite low amount of the 
required energy for the building should be covered at a significant extend by energy produced 
from renewable sources [1]. The nZEB requirement has to be implemented in either new 
building constructions or renovated existing buildings by the year 2019 [3]. However, EPBD 
do not provide a universal definition or a common approach to determine the construction 
choices or the calculation methodology of the energy use for the nZEB. Each country member 
has to determine the requirements for nZEB implementation into the building regulations [3]. 
In the study “Principles for Nearly Zero-Energy Buildings” by Buildings Performance 
Institute of Europe (BPIE) [3], ten challenges are extensively described and discussed in order 
to create a practical definition of nZEB. According to the study suggestions, there are three 
main principles in order to set up the appropriate definition of the nZEB. The first principle is 
that the energy demand of the building regarding the space heating and cooling, the domestic 
hot water and lighting should be clearly defined as well as how to assess the corresponding 
values [3]. The second principle is that the renewable energy share regarding the energy demand 
of the building should be calculated or measured with clear guidance [3]. Finally, the third 
principle for the nZEB universal definition is that there should be a clear guidance of how to 
calculate the primary energy use and what would be the CO2 emission targets [3].  
The suggestions of the Danish Government for nZEB are implemented in practice in the 
latest version of the Danish Building Regulations of 2015, in the section of Building class of 
2020 [4]. According to the regulations, there are various recommendations regarding the 
construction of the building envelope and the implementation of the building services in order 
the annual demand for energy supply for heating, ventilation, cooling and domestic hot water, 
not to exceed 20 kWh/m2 of heated floor area for dwellings and 25 kWh/m2 of heated floor area 
for office buildings [4]. 
==PAGE:8==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
8 
 
However, except the recommendations for reducing the energy demand of the building 
sector, buildings are designed to protect the occupants from the ambient climate conditions and 
provide them a secure indoor climate that fulfils their needs for an acceptable indoor air quality 
of the inhaled air and their needs for optimal thermal sensation through acceptable thermal 
comfort conditions. As a result, the design of high efficient buildings, except the low energy 
demand requirements has to be complied with the indoor climate requirements, as they are 
expressed by the European standards EN 15251 for categories I or II [5]. 
In order to implement the principles of nZEB into the building sector, a building system has 
to be developed. Building system is a term to describe the integration and the aggregation of all 
the parts of a building development procedure and their relation to the energy use and the indoor 
climate requirements. Parts of a building system are firstly the elements of the building 
structure, like the building geometry, the construction elements of the building envelope and 
the window elements, secondly the different building services, like the systems to provide space 
heating, cooling and ventilation but also the control strategies of all these systems and the 
occupancy type and schedule of the building. Each of these parts of the building system has to 
fulfill the design requirements and the individual energy use requirements, as they are defined 
in the building codes and regulations. The building system approach helps the designed to 
adjust several of the building design parameters, when it is possible based on the building 
regulations, in order to develop flexible building system solutions according to the needs of the 
different clients.    
Furthermore, the selection of the appropriate design approach has a severe influence in the 
energy requirements and the indoor climate conditions regarding the implementation of the 
nZEB principles in building systems. Most of the current design approaches for low energy 
buildings are primary to define the requirements for the energy use and the indoor climate and 
secondly to create multiple alternative building designs that fulfil the requirements they have 
been set [6]. If any of the several building designs do not fulfil the requirements may either 
rejected or adjusted afterwards. However, adjusting a building design to fulfil a specific target 
may compromise the fulfilment of other targets and more effort and time will be required from 
the designer to optimize it [6]. 
S. Petersen and S. Svendsen [6], proposed a new method for integrated design of building 
systems, by suggesting that the integrated design should be start from the room level, rather 
than the total building level. According to this method, several room designs of different 
geometries or orientations, which they are fulfill the principles of the nZEB in terms of energy 
use and indoor climate conditions, have to be created before the design of the whole building 
system [6]. It is more simple and more effective to design rooms to fulfill the specific 
requirements they have been set comparing to the whole building system [6]. There is no reason 
to define indoor climate conditions in a building system level, because each room may be 
different than the rest of the rooms in terms of orientation and occupancy [6]. If each room of 
the building system fulfills the requirements they have been set, the whole building system will 
fulfil also the requirements [6]. 
The most important part of the suggested design approach is the space of solutions for the 
integrated design of the building system. In the space of solution part of the design prosses, the 
several predefined room designs of different geometries and orientations, that fulfill the 
requirements of the nZEB, are selected and combined to a building system [6]. By using the 
space of solution, several building geometries can be created by selecting the desired number 
of predefined rooms for each geometry or orientation, in order to be used in several applications, 
according to the client needs [6]. The final building formations will always fulfill the 
requirements of nZEB as long as the selected room designs fulfil also the requirements.  
1.2. Aim of the project 
The aim of this project is to develop an office building system, based on the integrated 
design approach of the space of solutions, implementing the requirements of nZEB as they are 
==PAGE:9==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
9 
 
defined in the Building class of 2020 in the 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4] and the EN 
15251 standards [5]. 
The design of the building system will be start from the room level, by selecting the optimal 
room depth and the room geometry in general, based on the optimal workplace dimensions for 
three people offices rooms, in order to provide them the maximum satisfaction and improve 
their productivity level. Furthermore, the investigation in the room level will be focused on 
optimizing the construction of the office room regarding all the internal and external elements 
of the building envelope and implementing high efficient but also cost-effective solutions for 
the windows, the space heating and cooling systems and the ventilation system. Every room 
design has to fulfill the specific energy and indoor climate requirements of nZEB. In the end, 
several building system designs will be created by selecting the appropriate number of office 
rooms and they will be evaluated in terms of fulfilling the targets they have been set. 
The design and the investigation of the office building system will be performed by using 
the commercial software IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (IDA ICE) version 4.7.1 by EQUA.

Chapter 2: State-of-the-art 
2.1. introduction 
In this chapter, a definition of nZEB is provided along with some examples of contemporary, 
state of the art nZEB worldwide. Moreover, the main design characteristics of nZEB are 
presented and further analyzed. 
2.2. Definition and design characteristics of nZEB 
According to California Energy Commission, a nZEB is defined as “a building that merges 
highly energy-efficient building construction, state-of-the-art appliances and lighting systems 
and high-performance windows to reduce the building’s load and peak requirements. 
Additionally, a NZEB can include on-site solar water heating and RE, such as solar photovoltaic 
(PV), to meet its remaining energy needs.” This would result to a smart grid-connected building 
that could use energy from the grid as well as provide it back to the grid when there is a surplus 
of energy [7]. 
Nowadays, there are many examples of contemporary nZEB. The Science House at the 
Science Museum of Minnesota [8], [9], is a ZEB that consists of high-performance windows; 
daylight dimming controls, advanced overhangs, efficient insulation for the walls and roof, and 
advanced sensors for interior lighting. Furthermore, heating and cooling are provided by ground 
source heat pumps, Domestic Hot Water (DHW) as well as ventilation energy recovery are 
facilitated with the use of a heat pump. Last but not least, electricity is generated by integrated 
PV roofs of polycrystalline silicon panels. Another example of nZEB is the Hawaii Gateway 
Energy Center with an area of 334m2 built in 2005 [10], [9]. This building accomplished a 
combination of effective daylighting due to its orientation, a passive thermal chimney, and a 
cooling system that uses seawater to lower energy consumption. The building has achieved to 
export more electricity than it generates through a PV-integrated system. In addition, a mixed-
use (residential and commercial) nZEB are under construction in Masdar City in the United 
Arab Emirates. This project is going to encompass the world’s first large-scale positive-energy 
buildings. The buildings will include integrated wind turbines, building-integrated PV systems, 
outdoor air-quality monitoring and state of the art ventilation system. Natural daylight will be 
provided through cones and will support the buildings’ roofs by creating a shaded microclimate. 
Therefore, as explained in California Energy Commission’s definition of zero net energy 
building as well as other scientific sources, the main general design characteristics are the 
following [9]:  
a) 
Utilization of ‘smart energy management (automation, advanced sensors, heating and 
cooling control systems). 
b) 
Installation of energy-efficient heating, cooling and ventilation systems such as air-
conditioning with higher energy efficiency rating, solar air-conditioning, ground-source heat 
pumps that take advantage of geothermal energy, solar collectors and solar air-conditioning. 
c) 
Improvement of the building’s envelope (increase of building’s insulation and thermal 
mass, installation of cooling or phase change materials). 
d) 
Energy efficient lighting and appliances, that will try to minimize energy consumption. 
e) 
Integrated design such as interior space planning (optimal location a room in order to 
provide the maximum heat to the coolest place of the building), pioneering shading with 
properly designed window overhangs and advanced solar control windows.  
f) 
Use of renewable sources (installation of solar thermal systems, use of building-
integrated PVs and hybrid systems).

2.3. Integrated design of the building 
According to S. Petersen and S. Svendsen [6], the design and operational phase of a building 
is of great importance so as to accomplish high-energy efficiency of the building and minimize 
carbon emissions. In this work, an integrated design method is presented. This method proposes 
that the design of buildings should start on room level rather than entire building level and sets 
clear design goals concerning energy performance and indoor environment, accompanied by a 
building physical analysis of key performance indicators. This analysis is used to determine a 
space of solutions based on which the engineer can obtain useful information on the 
consequences of his design decisions as far as energy performance and indoor environment are 
concerned.  
Therefore, the engineer can create different room designs that accomplish the design goals 
prior to the entire building design. The various room designs can be combined in to a number 
of overall building designs from which the energy efficient design can be chosen and developed. 
Consequently, the final building design could achieve design goals since it will have an energy 
performance and indoor environment inherited from the room designs. According to the 
experiments’ results, the proposed method is the optimal method for achieving integrated 
design of low energy buildings with high quality indoor environment [6]. A simplified 
representation of the integrated design process used in this method is illustrated in  Figure 2.3.1. 
 
Figure 2.3.1. A simplified representation of the integrated design process [6]. 
2.4. Energy-efficient lighting system 
Lighting in commercial buildings is one of the greatest factors in workplace comfort and 
productivity. According to the literature [11], it is mentioned that daylight infiltration has 
positive effects on occupants’ comfort and has the ability to control daylight and save energy 
on electric lighting. In this study, a highly accurate illumination model was proposed to achieve 
the lighting requirements among the different spatial areas, while minimizing the energy needed 
to accomplish that. The proposed method is an illumination model-based method and an 
algorithm for intelligent open-loop lighting control. The results of the simulation method are 
presented using a simplistic virtual room model. According to the results, this methodology 
encompasses many advantages and it is concluded that daylight infiltration will be further 
addressed in the near future.  
Furthermore, according to another literature source [12], one of the most effective solutions 
in order to reduce carbon emissions and minimize energy costs in commercial buildings is to 
replace artificial lighting with natural daylighting. It is of critical importance to ensure that 
improper natural lighting or poor control doesn’t overheat the building or cause glare 
discomfort. Building Information Modeling (BIM) application is a new method that allows for 
the possibility of more integrated design since many design professionals can share information 
==PAGE:12==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
12 
 
and at the same time apply their specific knowledge to a single model. This new design method 
is contrasted to the more conventional one that depends on sequential hands offs from one 
specialization to the next. Additionally, BIM modeling is advantageous because it can minimize 
errors and design costs, as well as upgrade building performance by identifying ways and 
developing processes that could result in high performance buildings. 
2.5. Smart energy management 
According to a study [13], it is argued that design practices should be developed in order to 
be able to deal with performance uncertainty (e.g. deployment of climate conditions, 
consideration of various behavioral patterns and maximizing the performance of different 
technological components). Therefore, a framework for the study of Building Automation and 
Control Systems has been proposed. The target of this framework is to create an effective 
methodological continuity among building performance simulation, control and data analytics. 
Moreover, it targets at building a unified methodological approach, which could ensure many 
feedbacks from measured data, useful for the development of design as well as operation 
practices. Last but not least, this framework could assist the development of the entire building 
sector. 
2.6. Improvement of the building envelope 
In a literature review regarding the recent progress on net zero energy buildings [9], it is 
referred that thermal insulation materials can contribute to increasing the thermal comfort of a 
building without the use of extra heating. Thermal insulation materials met in the literature 
usually have thermal conductivity less than 0.13W/m2K. The following forms of insulating 
materials used in commercial buildings [14]:  
• 
mineral fiber blankets: batts and rolls (fiberglass and rock wool); 
• 
rigid boards (polystyrene, fiberglass); 
• 
foamed or sprayed in place (polyurethane and polyisocyanurate); 
• 
insulated concrete blocks; 
• 
insulated concrete form; 
• 
reflective materials (aluminium foil, ceramic coatings), which come as rolled foil, 
reflective paint, reflective metal shingles or foil-faced plywood sheathing; when reflective 
materials are placed on the roofs of a building they are known as ‘cool roofs’ and have the 
ability to reflect and emit radiation more effectively than conventional roofs. 
Moreover, in the same literature review [9], it is mentioned that another important envelope 
characteristic of nZEB are the windows. The windows are key elements because they have 
thermal conductivity around 5–10 times higher than the opaque envelope and also because they 
can allow daylight and solar radiation to enter the building. Therefore, in a study regarding zero 
energy windows [15], the following window technologies for nZEB are mentioned: highly 
insulating windows (aerogel, vacuum glazing), dynamic windows (passive dynamic glazing), 
active dynamic glazing and integrated facades for commercial buildings to control daylight. 
Furthermore, three factors can make a window very energy-efficient [16]: a) low-e coatings, b) 
gas filling and c) warm edge spacers. 
2.7. Energy-efficient heating, cooling and ventilation systems 
As far as heating is concerned, furnaces, boilers, packaged heating units, individual space 
heaters as well as heat pumps are currently being used for nZEB. Cooling systems include 
central air-conditioners, heat pumps (both ground and air-source), individual room air-
conditioners, district chilled water, central chillers, packaged air-conditioning units and swamp 
coolers [17]. Regarding ventilation systems, after thorough research in the literature [9], it is 
concluded that nZEB mostly use mechanical ventilation with heat recovery systems. In 
addition, buildings located in warmer climates utilize natural ventilation effectively throughout 
the day or night (night cooling) so as to cool the thermal mass of the building.

Chapter 3: Methodology 
3.1. Introduction 
The methodology in order to develop an office building system, based on the integrated 
design approach of space of solutions and implementing the requirements of nZEB, as they are 
defined in the Building class of 2020 in the 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4] and the 
indoor climate requirements based on EN 15251 standard [5], was an extended parameter 
investigation process regarding several aspects of the design procedure. This investigation 
prosses was performed by using the commercial software IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (IDA 
ICE) version 4.7.1 by EQUA.  
The geometry of the building system, the number and the arrangement of the workplaces 
and several others supporting areas inside the building, like the corridors or meeting rooms, 
were selected and varied in order to provide flexibility and to fulfil the specific requirements of 
different clients.  
The glazing areas and the properties of the window elements on the façades were varied and 
their effect regarding the minimum Daylight Factor (DF) in the permanent workplaces and the 
primary energy use for artificial lighting of the building system was evaluated. Also, the 
lighting system was designed and the lighting control strategy was optimized in order to provide 
supplementary artificial lighting to the available daylight level, to minimize the primary energy 
use for lighting.  
The construction of the façade element of the building envelope was evaluated and improved 
in order to create a lightweight and high insulated façade solution, based on the prefabricated 
construction prosses, with the minimum thickness, in order the minimum DF inside the building 
module to be increased and the primary energy use for lighting to be reduced. 
In addition, the heating and cooling requirements of the building system were identified in 
order the design requirements of the indoor environment, as they are expressed in EN 15251 
[5], to be fulfilled. To reduce the energy requirements for cooling, different passive solutions 
were implemented and investigated. External blinds or glazing units with solar control coating 
were used as a supplementary solution to the main mechanical cooling system. However, the 
implementation of these passive cooling systems affected the primary energy use for heating 
and artificial lighting. The optimal relation between these three systems was investigated. 
Finally, the ventilation system of the building system was optimized based on the Demand 
Controlled Ventilation (DCV) principles, according to the requirements of the 2015 Danish 
Building Regulations [4] and EN 15251 [5]. In addition, the number of the occupants in the 
office building system was varied in order to detect the relation between the reduction of the 
occupancy level with the reduction of the overall primary energy use.    
3.2. Energy use and indoor climate main requirements 
According to 2015 Danish Building Regulations for the  Building class of 2020, the 
following design requirement for primary energy use is stated: “Offices, schools, institutions 
and other buildings not covered by 7.2.4.2 may be classified as building class 2020 when the 
total demand for energy supply for heating, ventilation, cooling, domestic hot water and lighting 
per m² heated floor area does not exceed 25 kWh/m² per year” [4]. 
Regarding the Daylight Factor (DF) requirements, it is stated that: “Daylight in workrooms 
etc. may similarly be taken to be sufficient if a calculation or measurement can show that there 
is a daylight factor of 2% at the workspaces” [4]. However, for buildings that are categorized 
in the building class of 2020, it is stated that: “daylight levels may be considered satisfactory if 
the daylight factors for the rooms/spaces are higher than 3% at the workspaces as documented 
by calculation” [4] 
Regarding the indoor climate design requirements, the category II of indoor environment, 
according to EN 15251 [5] was used. The design operative temperature range should be from 
==PAGE:14==

Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007

14 
 
20oC to 24oC for heating operation and from 23oC to 26oC for cooling operation. Regarding the 
acceptable overheating hours, in Danish Standards DS 469 [18], it is stated that mechanical 
cooling can be installed only if it is not possible to reduce the indoor temperature by any other 
mean, in order to limit the indoor temperature above 26oC for only 100 h and above 27oC for 
only 25 h. In Danish Building Regulations of 2015 it is stated that: “For buildings other than 
dwellings, the client determines the maximum number of hours per year during which an indoor 
temperature of 26°C and 27°C, respectively, may be exceeded” [4].  
The ventilation air flow rate (l/s) for the category II of indoor environment [5], it was 
calculated as the sum of the ventilation air flow rate for occupants, which should be 7 l/s/person 
and the ventilation rate for diluting the building emissions, which should be 0.7 l/s/m2 of floor 
area, for low polluting buildings.  
Furthermore, as the CO2 concentration (ppm) is one of the most important indoor 
contaminant related to the occupants behavior, EN 15251 defines the maximum recommended 
indoor concentration of CO2 at 500 ppm above outdoors [5]. Also, in the 2015 Danish Building 
Regulations for the building class of 2020 it is stated that: “It must be ensured that the CO2 
content of the indoor air does not exceed 900 ppm for extended periods of time” [4].  
The primary energy factors used to calculate the primary energy use from the delivered 
energy used in the different building systems, were according to the 2015 Danish Building 
Regulations for the Building class of 2020. According to the regulations, the primary energy 
factor for electricity was 1.8 and the primary energy factor for district heating was 0.6 [4] the 
primary energy factor for energy based on Renewable Energy Sources (RES) was 0. 
Except from the main requirements for the primary energy use, the indoor climate and the 
sufficient daylight, as they were defined previously, there were also technical recommendations 
regarding the design of the building system that were not part of the investigation process. All 
these technical recommendations were according to Danish Building Regulations of 2015 [4] 
and they were remained constant through the different phases of the investigation procedure. 
All these recommendations are analytically presented and described in the relevant chapters, 
where the different phases of the investigation process are described. 
3.3. Reference building system 
All the different investigated parameters as they were described in section 3.1, would create 
an extremely high number of different variation cases of the building system to be examined, 
which was not desirable. The purpose of this investigation process was to identify the effect 
and the limitations of each parameter, in relation to the total primary energy use and the indoor 
climate conditions, in order to be implemented in the office building system. As a result, a 
reference case of the building system was created as a start of the investigation process. 
Due to the desired flexibility of the office building system regarding the different client 
requirements, five building variations were created as reference cases. Four of them were 
arrangements of landscape office systems of 10 m, 13 m, 15 m and 18 m building width and 
the last of them was an arrangement of single man office rooms system of 10 m building width. 
The four-different landscape office building systems were varied between them regarding the 
number and the arrangement of the workplaces. To simplify the investigation procedure taking 
into consideration the space of solution approach, all the five reference building system 
variations were examined and modeled as rectangular symmetrically “sliced” modules in IDA 
ICE. In the five reference building modules were created, both the façade length and room 
height was 3 m. The two façades were oriented in the South and the North side of the modules 
respectively. The floor and the ceiling of the building modules were considered as the same 
adiabatic internal floor/ceiling element. The side walls of the landscape variations modules 
were also considered adiabatic internal wall elements. However, in the landscape building 
modules they should not be existed in real conditions. Due to the limitation of IDA ICE to 
eliminate them, the internal wall elements were designed with mirror inner surfaces, in order to 
==PAGE:15==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
15 
 
reflect the daylight in the same way as the daylight would distributed into the building if they 
were not existed. 
In all the five reference buildings modules, initial selections regarding the glazing areas, the 
window properties, the artificial lighting design, the construction layers of the building 
envelope elements, the heating and cooling system, the passive cooling solutions and the 
ventilation system were implemented. 
Regarding the window elements, a commercial tilt-turn window product was selected. The 
width of the window frame on the façade was quite thin, in order to maximize the light 
transmittance through the window element. The glazing area was selected in order to improve 
the daylight performance of the building modules and to provide sufficient outside view to the 
occupants. The minimum DF in the permanent workplaces was identified based on daylight 
simulations through IDA ICE, in order the minimum DF in the critical workplaces to be higher 
than 2%. 
The artificial lighting design was performed for every workplace, corridor or meeting room 
individually. High efficient LED luminaries were selected in order to provide the exact 
illuminance level in the zones. In the workplaces and the meeting rooms, the illuminance set 
point was 500 lx while in the corridors the illuminance set point was 100 lx, according to EN 
12464-1 [19]. The initial control strategy of the lighting system was a simple ON/OFF function, 
according to the illuminance level due to the daylight which it was detected by a light sensor at 
the position of the first occupant, based on a simple Proportional-Integral (PI) controller. As 
long as the illuminance level due to the daylight was below the set point value, the lighting 
system was operating at the nominal power, providing 500 lx or 100 lx to the zone. If the 
illuminance level due to the daylight is equal or above the illuminance set point of the zone, the 
lighting system was deactivated. 
The elements of the building envelope, such as the external walls, the internal walls and the 
internal floor/ceiling element, were constructed in order to be lightweight and high insulated. 
As a result, the construction materials and the thickness of the layers in every element of the 
building envelope were selected based on this principal in order the overall U-value (W/m2K) 
of every element to be according to the requirements of  2015 Danish Building Regulations [4]. 
Ideal heaters and coolers were used in order to model the mechanical heating and cooling 
system of the five-reference system. They were used in order to detect and provide the heating 
and cooling requirements. These systems were selected in order to provide to the different 
clients the possibility of using the heating and cooling system of their choice. The heating and 
cooling set points were based on the EN 15251 [5] standards. 
Regarding the supplementary passive cooling systems, external blinds were used as a 
shading system, on every window element in order to minimize the solar gains. The operation 
of the external blinds was based on the measured solar irradiation level on the outer side of the 
window element. If the solar irradiation was higher than 100 W/m2, the external blinds were 
drawn. 
For the ventilation system, a Constant Air Volume (CAV) strategy was selected for every 
zone of the building module. The maximum and minimum ventilation flow rates (l/sm2) were 
calculated for every zone of the five reference building modules according to EN 15251 [5] 
requirements. The minimum ventilation flow rate of 0.7 l/s/m2 of floor area for the building 
emissions was provided into the building modules during both occupied and unoccupied hours. 
In Chapter 4: Reference building system, the creation of the five reference building modules 
in IDA ICE is described in detail. The initial values and solutions regarding the design 
parameters of the investigation process were selected and described. All the supplementary 
design requirements that were constant during the investigation process were selected based on 
the 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4] and EN 15251 standard [5]. The compliance with the 
design requirements was evaluated based on the results of the IDA ICE simulation.

3.4. Daylight and lighting 
The most important element of the investigation procedure was the daylight analysis and the 
lighting design. The sufficient daylight level inside the building, except from the advantages 
related to the productivity of the occupants, can significantly reduce the primary energy use for 
artificial lighting. The daylight level inside a building can be described by the minimum 
Daylight Factor (DF) (%) inside every workplace and supporting area of the building. The 
parameters that affected the DF in every workplace were evaluated in this part of the 
investigation process. These parameters were the total width (m) of the building system, the 
number of the permanent workplaces close to the façades, the glazing area (m2) of the windows 
elements on both façades, the Light Transmittance (LT) (%) of the glazing units and the frame 
area (m2) in relation to the total window area (m2) on the façades. 
In order to identify the effect of the parameters related to the available daylight level inside 
the building, a wide daylight analysis investigation process was performed. In every of the five 
building modules of different widths and workplaces arrangements, variation cases were 
created regarding the window elements on the façade. In every different window variation case 
that it was created, the minimum DF in every permanent workplace was calculated based on 
IDA ICE simulations.   
For the creation of the window variation cases, the size, the number and the type of the 
window elements on the façades were varied. Also, the implementation of solar control glazing 
units as a passive cooling solution was examined in relation to the daylight performance. 
Finally, a variation case was created by using high insulated 4-pane glazing units. 
Different window arrangements were created on both façades by using one or more than one 
identical window elements. Regarding the different sizes of the window arrangement, the length 
of the window elements was varied while the height was constant. In all the different window 
variation cases that were created, the window elements were placed in the center of the façade. 
The maximum length of the window arrangements was 3 m as the maximum available length 
of the façade element. The lower acceptable length of the window arrangements was selected 
in order the minimum DF of 2% [4] to be fulfilled in the critical workplaces. The reduction step 
of the window arrangement length was 0.2 m. The height of the window elements was selected 
to be 2 m and they were placed at 1 m height from the floor level. The height and the position 
of the window elements related to the floor level was selected in order sufficient outside view 
to be provided to the occupants, water radiators to be installed below the window elements and 
the daylight level in the more centralized areas of the building to be maximized. 
In addition, two different window types were used. The first window type was the same tilt-
turn window unit of the reference building modules, as it is presented in Chapter 4: Reference 
building system. However, regarding tilt-turn window product, there were some limitation 
regarding the available window lengths for the selected constant window height of 2 m. To 
overcome these limitations, multiple identical tilt-turn window units were used in a row in every 
different window arrangement variation. 
Except from the tilt-turn window unit, a fixed window unit was also used in the different 
window variation cases, by the same manufacturer. This fixed window element was identical 
to the tilt-turn window type regarding the frame construction and the properties of the glazing 
unit. The reason for the use of the fixed window element was that there was a wider variety of 
available window lengths for the constant window height of 2 m. As a result, by using this 
window type instead of the tilt-turn version, the number of the window units per façade could 
be minimized along with the total frame area. 
The default glazing unit for both the tilt-turn and fixed window type was created by three 
glass panes of 4 mm thickness and two 90% Argon gas filled cavities of 18 mm between them. 
However, according to GLASFAKTA 2015 [20], the thickness of the outer glass pane should 
not be lower than 6 mm in the window units for a constant height at 2 m and length higher than 
1.8 m, in order to resist against the wind pressure. As a result, for the window variation cases 
==PAGE:17==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
17 
 
where the length of the window elements was higher than 1.8 m, it was assumed that a special 
enhanced glazing unit construction of the selected window product could be created, replacing 
the initial glazing unit, from a different glazing manufacturer.  
In addition, for the window variation cases of 3 m length of both tilt-turn and fixed window 
type, the implementation of a high insulated 4-pane glazing unit was examined. Due to the fact 
that the manufacturer of the selected window products did not provide the possibility of using 
glazing units more than three glass panes, a new 4-pane glazing unit was constructed by the 
same manufacturer as the enhanced glazing unit. However, the thickness of the 4-pane glazing 
unit was higher than the default 3-pane glazing unit. Taking into account that the frame element 
was created to accept glazing units with thickness as the default 3-pane unit, it was assumed 
that the frame construction of the selected window products could be extended in order to accept 
the thickness of the 4-pane glazing unit, without compromising the U-value (W/m2K) of the 
frame element. 
Although, it would be interesting to examine the possibility of using glazing units with solar 
control coating as a solution to reduce the primary energy use for cooling, by reducing the solar 
gains through the window elements. However, the manufacturer of the selected window 
products did not provide the opportunity to use alternative glazing units with solar control 
coating. To overcame this limitation, once again it was assumed that a special construction of 
the default glazing unit, the enhanced glazing unit and the 4-pane glazing unit could be created 
for the selected window products, by replacing default outer glass pane with a glass pane with 
solar control coating. The creation of the solar control glazing units was based on the same 
glazing manufacturer as the enhanced and the 4-pane glazing units.  
The glazing units with solar control coating of lower LT value were implemented in the 
default tilt-turn and fixed window elements in all the different window variation cases created. 
However, the lower LT of the glazing units with solar control coating could reduce the 
minimum DF in all the permanent workplaces and the primary energy use for lighting would 
be increased.  
The creation of the window arrangement variations of different length regarding the two-
different window types and the creation of the different glazing units are described in detail in 
Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting, in Appendix A: Window variation cases and in Appendix B: 
Solar control glazing unit. The minimum DF results in every permanent workplace of all the 
five different building modules, regarding every different window variation case created, were 
calculated based on IDA ICE simulations. The results are also presented in Chapter 5: Daylight 
and lighting. 
Once the daylight analysis results were obtained for every different window variation case 
examined and for all the five different building modules, the artificial lighting design was 
performed. The artificial lighting design was performed only for the window variation cases 
that according to the daylight analysis results they fulfilled the minimum DF requirements of 
2%, as they were defined in 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4]. In the investigation process 
regarding the lighting design, the primary energy use for artificial lighting was identified 
regarding the different building width, the different glazing area, the different LT of the glazing 
unit and the different illuminance set point value. 
Concerning the illuminance level, 500 lx were required in the workplaces and meeting 
rooms and 100 lx were required in the corridor, according to DS/EN 12464-1 [19]. However, 
based on the description in the standard, this level of illuminance is mainly for “Writing, typing, 
reading, data processing” [19]. Nowadays, most of the office work is performed through 
personal computers or laptops instead of paper work. All these electronic devices are using 
screens that they are providing a supplementary artificial light, which is enough in order the 
office tasks to be performed. Taking into account that the primary energy use for artificial 
lighting is a an important share of the total primary energy use of the office building system, it 
would be interesting to identify the reduction of the primary energy use for lighting if the 
illuminance set point could be reduced from 500 lx to 300 lx in the workplaces and meeting 
==PAGE:18==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
18 
 
rooms. The illuminance set point at the corridors was always 100 lx. As a result, for every 
different window variation case created in all the five building modules, two more variations 
were created regarding the operation of the lighting system, based on the two-different 
illuminance set point values of 500 lx and 300 lx in the workplaces and meeting rooms. 
The operating schedule of the lighting system was the same as the occupancy schedule of 
the building. Furthermore, in order to minimize the primary energy use for artificial lighting, 
high efficient LED luminaries were used in every variation.  
Regarding the control strategy of the lighting system, in the reference building modules in 
Chapter 4: Reference building system, a simple ON/OFF controller was used based on the 
daylight level and the illuminance set point. However, this control strategy of providing always 
either 0 lx or 500 lx from the lighting system, even if there was available daylight in the 
workplaces but lower than the set point illuminance, was not energy efficient.  
As a result, a new energy efficient control strategy was created, in order to minimize the 
primary energy use for artificial lighting. In this new control strategy, the dimming function of 
the installed lighting luminaries was used through a proportional controller, in order to provide 
supplementary artificial lighting to the daylight level, so as the illuminance level to be always 
equal to the set point value. When the illuminance level due to the daylight was higher than the 
illuminance set point value in the workplaces, meeting rooms or corridors, the lighting system 
was completely closed. In the case of 0 lx due to the daylight, the lighting system was operating 
in the maximum nominal power, as it was defined by the set point value.  
The available daylight level was detected by the same light sensors placed in every zone of 
the building, as they were used in the initial control strategy of the reference building modules. 
The external blinds were never drawn in this part of the investigation process, in order to 
identify the highest potential regarding the reduction of the primary energy use for lighting by 
using the energy efficient lighting control strategy for the two-different illuminance set point. 
In Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting and in Appendix C: Luminaries selection, the number 
and the characteristics of the LED luminaries used in every workplace, meeting room or 
corridor, for the two-different illuminance set point values are described in detail. In addition, 
the new energy efficient control strategy of the lighting system is presented in detail. Finally, 
the results regarding the primary energy use for lighting are presented based on IDA ICE 
dynamic simulations, regarding every different window variation of the five different building 
modules and for the two-different illuminance set points values. 
3.5. Improvement of the façade wall element 
After the daylight analysis and lighting design, the investigation process was focused on 
improving the wall façade element. Even if the initial construction of the façade element was 
lightweight and high insulated, based on the prefabrication principles, the total thickness of the 
façade element was quite high. The reduction of the thickness of the façade element could 
increase the minimum DF in the permanent workplaces and reduce the primary energy use for 
lighting. 
As a result, the new façade wall element should be also high insulated and light weight, 
based on prefabrication principles, but the overall thickness should be quite lower. Based on 
research regarding several commercial products, a façade panel was selected by the same 
manufacturer as the window elements used. This new façade element was implemented in the 
façade construction as spandrel panels between the window elements of a multi-storey office 
building system. The default construction of the façade panel allowed to implement additional 
insulation layer in order to reduce the U-value, close to the U-value of the reference façade wall 
element. However, the total thickness of the spandrel façade panel was less than half comparing 
to the initial thickness of the reference wall element. 
When the new façade panels were implemented in the five building modules for all the 
different window variation cases and the different glazing units, once again a daylight analysis 
==PAGE:19==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
19 
 
was performed in order to identify the increase of the minimum DF in every permanent 
workplace and to detect the new minimum acceptable glazing area in the different modules.  
In addition, a new calculation procedure through IDA ICE simulations was performed in 
order to identify the reduction of the primary energy use for artificial lighting for all the 
different window variation cases and the different glazing units in the five building modules, 
due to the use of the new façade panels of lower thickness. This calculation procedure was 
performed based on the new energy efficient lighting control strategy and only for the 
illuminance set point of 500 lx. In the cases of the glazing units without solar control coating 
the external blinds were never drawn in order to identify the difference only due to the change 
of the façade element. 
The detail description of the new façade spandrel panel, the implementation on the façade 
element regarding the different window variation cases and the results regarding the new 
daylight analysis and the calculations of the primary energy use for artificial lighting are 
presented in Chapter 6: Improved façade element. 
3.6. Primary energy use and indoor climate evaluation 
After the implementation of the improved façade element in every window variation case 
of the five building modules, the investigation procedure was focused on the evaluation of the 
overall primary energy use for lighting, heating, cooling, ventilation and Domestic Hot Water 
(DHW), but also on the evaluation of the indoor climate conditions, in all the accepted window 
variation cases, according to the minimum DF results of Chapter 6: Improved façade element. 
In order to minimize the primary energy use for ventilation, a new ventilation strategy was 
implemented in the five reference building modules. This ventilation strategy was based on the 
Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) principles, based on the CO2 concentration (ppm) level 
inside the zones, through a Variable Air Volume (VAV) ventilation system. In order to model 
this ventilation strategy in IDA ICE models, the default “VAV strategy based on CO2” control 
in IDA ICE was selected in the different building modules. The CO2 concentration level of 850 
ppm was selected as the minimum level that the maximum ventilation rate would be provided, 
in order the CO2 concentration in all the zones of the different building modules no to exceed 
900 ppm. The ventilation rates that were calculated and used in every zone of the five building 
modules, according to EN 15251 [5] in Chapter 4: Reference building system, were also used 
as the maximum and minimum ventilation rates after the implementation of the DCV strategy.  
The ventilation system was used only in order to provide fresh air into the occupants. As a 
result, the operation of the ventilation system was depended only on the number of occupants 
which it was remained constant in all the different window variation cases. As a result, the 
primary energy use for ventilation would be the same in all the different window variation cases 
of the same building module. 
The same ideal heaters and coolers with the same control strategies that used in the reference 
building modules and they were described in Chapter 4: Reference building system, were also 
used in this part of the investigation process. Also, the same internal gains related to the number 
of occupants and equipment were used. Regarding the operation of the lighting system the new 
energy efficient control strategy was used and the illuminance set point was set to 500 lx in the 
workplaces and meeting rooms.  
Two different passive cooling solutions of external blinds as a shading system and glazing 
units with solar control coating were implemented in the different window variation cases of 
the five building modules. In the window variation cases that glazing units without solar control 
coating were used, the operation of the external blinds as the shading system was activated in 
order to minimize the primary energy use for cooling.  
In the investigation process that it was performed in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting and in 
Chapter 6: Improved façade element, the purpose was to identify the reduction of the primary 
energy use for lighting, only due to the reduction of the illuminance set point value or the 
==PAGE:20==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
20 
 
reduction of the thickness of the facade element, eliminating any additional effect due to the 
external blinds operation. In this part of the investigation process that the overall primary energy 
use of the building modules was evaluated, the use of the external blinds would increase the 
primary energy use for lighting comparing to the results in Chapter 6: Improved façade element, 
in order the reduction of the solar gains to be achieved. As a result, in this part of the 
investigation process the evaluation of the primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling 
due to the use of the external blinds and the glazing units with solar control coating was 
performed. 
The control of the shading devices was based on the same strategy that it was described in 
Chapter 4: Reference building system. According to this strategy, the solar irradiation on the 
outer side of the window elements was detected and if it was exceeding 100 W/m2, the external 
blinds were drawn. However, due to the difference of the building width in the five building 
modules, the set point of 100 W/m2, would be the optimal for the building modules of lower 
width, but it would increase significant the primary energy use for lighting in the wider building 
modules. As a result, before the evaluation of the overall primary energy use in the variation 
cases that the external blinds were used, an investigation process was performed in order to 
identify the optimal irradiation set point value for the external blinds operation in the five 
building modules. The setpoint value that resulted in to lower primary energy use for lighting, 
heating and cooling, was selected as the setpoint value in all the different window variation 
cases in the same building module. 
Regarding the window variation cases that the solar control glazing units were used, the 
results regarding the primary energy use for lighting was the same as in Chapter 6: Improved 
façade element. As a result, in this part of the investigation procedure, the primary energy use 
for heating and cooling was identified. 
The detailed description of the investigation procedure in order to evaluate the primary 
energy use for lighting, heating, cooling, ventilation and DHW as it was described in this 
section, along with the primary energy use results and the indoor climate conditions for all the 
different variation cases examined, are presented in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor 
climate. 
3.7. Sensitivity analysis 
In the final part of the investigation procedure, four different sensitivity analyses were 
performed, regarding different investigation parameters. 
In the first sensitivity analysis, the number of the occupants in the workplaces was reduced. 
Even if the office building systems were designed for a maximum number of occupants, in real 
conditions, the number of occupants who are present in the office every day is usually lower 
than the designed maximum number. This is due to the random absence of different occupants 
in different days. Also, the holiday period of the office workers was not taken into account in 
the occupancy schedule. However, there was a limitation in order to model in IDA ICE the 
random behavior of the office occupants during the different days. 
As a result, it was decided to reduce the total number of occupants in the five building 
modules, regarding all the different window variation cases, either with the use of shading 
system or the glazing units with solar control coating. Two variation cases were created; one 
with 75% occupancy and another one with 50% occupancy, comparing to the default 100% 
number of occupants, as it was described in Chapter 4: Reference building system. In order to 
model the reduction of the number of occupants, taking into account that the office building 
system was examined in “sliced” building modules of 3 m façade length, it was decided to 
decrease the number of occupants in every workplace from 1 in the default occupancy of 100% 
to 0.75 and 0.5 regarding the two different occupancy level variations of 75% and 50% 
respectively. This unnatural reduction in the number of occupants was decided in order the 
occupants to be equally distributed into the building modules.  
==PAGE:21==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
21 
 
Except from the number of occupants, the number of equipment was reduced also in the 
same pattern, in order to be in relation with the number of occupants. The rest of the input 
parameters regarding the different systems of the building modules like the lighting, the 
heating, the cooling and the ventilation systems were remained constant in relation to the 
investigation process it was performed for the default number of occupants in Chapter 7: 
Primary energy use and indoor climate.  
Once again, a supplementary investigation process was performed through IDA ICE 
simulations in order to identify the effect of a lower occupancy level to the primary energy use 
for heating, cooling and ventilation. Also, the results regarding the indoor climate conditions 
were illustrated. 
In the second sensitivity analysis, in order to reduce the primary energy use for ventilation, 
in the Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) system, the minimum ventilation rate of 0.7 l/sm2 
during the unoccupied hours was reduced to 0.1 l/sm2, according to the suggestions of EN 
15251 [5]. This reduction in the minimum ventilation rate, affected also the primary energy use 
for heating and cooling due to the reduced air changes per hour (ach) during the unoccupied 
hours. As a result, the age of air (h) was increased and the solar gains from the window elements 
increased the mean air temperature and as a result the balance between the primary energy use 
for heating and cooling was different. 
In order to identify the reduction in the primary energy use for ventilation and the differences 
in the primary energy use for heating and cooling, a supplementary investigation process was 
performed in the five building modules regarding all the window variation cases, either with 
the implementation of the external blinds or the use of the glazing units with solar control 
coating. The maximum ventilation rates were not changed, the designed occupancy level of 
100% was remained and the rest of the input parameters were according to the investigation 
process in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate. 
In the third sensitivity analysis, based on the results of Chapter 7: Primary energy use and 
indoor climate, for the window variation cases with solar control coating and for the default 
number of occupants, the glazing units with solar control coating were improved in order the 
solar gains through the window elements to be reduced. 
In order to improve the glazing units with solar control coating to achieve a further reduction 
of the solar gains, the solar heat gain coefficient (g) of the glazing units should be further 
reduced. As a result, the glazing units were created in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting were 
adjusted by replacing the outer glass panes with solar control coating with a different one in 
order the solar heat gain coefficient (g) to be reduced. Four new variations of glazing units with 
solar control coating were created. The two variations of the higher solar heat gain coefficient 
(g) were implemented in the landscape building module of the higher width, while the two 
variations of the lower solar heat gain coefficient (g) were implemented in the rest of the 
landscape building modules and the single man office building module. 
Except form the reduction of the solar heat gain coefficient (g), the LT of the glazing units 
was also reduced. Due to the reduction of the LT value, a new daylight analysis was performed 
in order to identify the minimum acceptable window variation cases in the five building 
modules. In order to identify the optimal relation between the reduction of the primary energy 
use for cooling, due to the decreased of the solar heat gain coefficient (g), and the increase of 
the primary energy use for lighting, due to the reduction of the LT of the glazing unit, an 
investigation process was performed in the five reference building modules for all the 
acceptable window variation cases, based on the new daylight results. All the input parameters 
of the lighting, heating, cooling and ventilation system were according to Chapter 7: Primary 
energy use and indoor climate. 
In the last sensitivity analysis, the position of the examined building modules was varied in 
a multi-storey office building system. During the investigation process so far, it was assumed 
that the examined building modules were in the middle of the multi-storey office building 
==PAGE:22==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
22 
 
system. As a result, there were transmition heat losses only through the two external façade 
elements, taking into account that the rest of the internal elements were adiabatic surfaces. 
As a base for this sensitivity analysis, the five building modules with the same window 
arrangement variation of 2.6 m length were used. For this window variation case, the glazing 
unit without solar control coating combined with the operation of the external blinds was also 
used. The new façade panels were used for the construction of the façade element. Three 
different variations for each of the five building modules were created. 
In the first variation case, one of the two side walls of the five modules was replaced by the 
default external wall element as it was described in Chapter 4: Reference building system. As 
a result, the mirror inner surface of the side walls was replaced by the default inner surface of 
the wall elements in the four landscape building modules. A new energy calculation through 
IDA ICE simulations was performed in order to identify the increase in the primary energy use 
for lighting due to the reduction of the reflectance of the inner surface of the side wall and the 
difference in the primary energy use for heating and cooling due to the additional transmition 
losses through the external side wall area. 
In the second variation case, the internal ceiling element of the five modules was replaced 
by new introduced external roof element. A new energy calculation through IDA ICE 
simulations was performed in order to identify the difference in the primary energy use for 
heating and cooling due to the additional transmition losses through the external roof area. 
Finally, in the third variation case, the internal floor element of the five modules was 
replaced by new introduced external floor element.  A new energy calculation through IDA ICE 
simulations was performed in order to identify the difference in the primary energy use for 
heating and cooling due to the additional transmition losses through the external floor area. 
The detailed description of the four sensitivity analyses along with the results regarding the 
primary energy use are presented in Chapter 8: Sensitivity analysis.

Chapter 4: Reference building system 
4.1. Introduction 
In this Chapter, the detail model construction of the five reference building systems is 
described. Four of the five reference building systems were landscape offices while the last one 
was a building system of single man office rooms. Initial solutions and reference values 
regarding all the investigated parameters were selected and implemented into the modes, along 
with several other technical requirements, based on the 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4], 
that were not part of the investigation process and they were constant throughout the 
investigation process.  
The geometry and the arrangement of the workplaces, corridors or meeting rooms in every 
of the five-reference building system are described analytically. Specific solutions were used 
for the daylight and lighting design, the window elements, the construction of elements of the 
building envelope, the space heating and cooling systems and the ventilation system. 
Finally, the primary energy use for all the five building system variations was calculated 
and evaluated based on the requirements of 2015 Danish Building Regulations of 2020 building 
energy class [4] and the indoor clime conditions were evaluated based on the EN 15251 
requirements [5] through IDA ICE simulations. 
4.2. The geometry, the arrangement of workplaces, corridors and meeting rooms and the 
construction of the reference building system 
As it was described in Chapter 3: Methodology, five different reference building variations 
were created in IDA ICE. The four of them were landscape office variations of different 
building widths and workplace arrangements and the last of them was a variation of single man 
office rooms. All the different building variations were examined as symmetrical office 
modules of 3 m façade length and 3 m room height. The two façades were oriented in the South 
and the North direction of the building modules. The rest of the module elements like the 
ceiling, the floor and the side walls were internal elements. 
In the first landscape building variation, the building width was 10 m and the number of the 
zones created in IDA ICE was five. There were two workplaces of 2 m width in a row close to 
each of the two façades of the building and there was a corridor of 2 m width in the middle of 
the building. There were no partition walls between the four workplaces and the corridor and 
all the internal floor area of the module was considered as an open plan area. The reference 
landscape building module of 10 m width is illustrated in Figure 4.2.1. 
 
Figure 4.2.1. Landscape office reference building variation of 10 m building width as it is illustrated in IDA 
ICE floor plan. 
In the second landscape building variation, the building width was 13 m and the number of 
the zones created in IDA ICE was seven. There were three workplaces of 1.8 m width in a row 
close to each of the two façades of the building and there was a corridor of 2.2 m width in the 
middle of the building. Also in this variation, there were no partition walls between the six 
workplaces and the corridor and all the internal floor area of the module was considered as an 
==PAGE:24==

open plan area. The reference landscape building module of 13 m width is illustrated in Figure 
4.2.2. 
 
Figure 4.2.2. Landscape office reference building variation of 13 m building width as it is illustrated in IDA 
ICE floor plan. 
In the third landscape building variation, the building width was 15 m and the number of the 
zones created in IDA ICE was seven. There were two workplaces of 1.8 m width in a row close 
to each of the two façades of the building, a meeting room of 3.8 m width in the center of the 
building and there were two corridors of 2 m width between the two workplaces close to the 
façades and the central meeting room. The meeting room in the center of the building was 
separated from the rest of the building area by internal glazed walls. Between the two 
workplaces close to each of the two facades and the two corridors, there were no partition walls 
and as a result, these two areas between each of the two façades and the meeting room in the 
middle there were considered as open plan areas. The reference landscape building module of 
15 m building width is illustrated in Figure 4.2.3. 
 
Figure 4.2.3. Landscape office reference building variation of 15 m building width as it is illustrated in IDA 
ICE floor plan. 
In the fourth landscape building variation, the building width was 18 m and the number of 
the zones created in IDA ICE was nine. There were three workplaces of 1.8 m width in a row 
close to each of the two façades of the building, a meeting room of 3.6 m width in the center of 
the building and there were two corridors of 1.8 m width between the two workplaces close to 
the façades and the central meeting room. Like the reference building system of 15 m width, 
the meeting room in the center of the building was separated from the rest of the building area 
by internal glazed walls. Between the two workplaces close to each of the two facades and the 
two corridors, there were no partition walls and as a result, these two areas between each of the 
two façades and the meeting room in the middle there were considered as open plan areas. The 
reference landscape building module of 18 m building width is illustrated in Figure 4.2.4.

4.3. Construction of the building envelope elements in the reference building modules 
The examined reference building modules were consisted of external wall elements in both 
façades, while the rest of the construction elements such as the ceiling, the floor and the rest of 
the wall elements were internal elements. The internal floor and internal ceiling elements were 
the same internal slab element. In the case of the internal floor, the inner surface was the top 
surface of the slab element, while in the case of the internal ceiling, the inner surface was the 
bottom surface of the slab element.  
Due to the investigation of the building systems as building modules of only 3 m façade 
length, the internal construction elements that were not adjusted to the faces of the building 
envelope, like the side internal walls, the internal floor element and the internal ceiling element, 
they were considered in IDA ICE as adiabatic surfaces by default. However, all the internal 
elements that were connected into multiple zones like the partition walls between the zones 
were not considered as adiabatic surfaces. 
All the different elements were constructed in order to be lightweight and high insulated. 
The construction materials of the external wall elements along with their thermal properties are 
presented in Table 4.3.1. The properties of the material used were according to the IDA ICE 
default material library and based on the 62 [21].
