In the landscape building modules of 15 m and 18 m width, the meeting rooms were 
separated from the two corridors by internal glazing wall constructions. It was assumed that for 
the examined building modules of 3 m façade length, the internal glazing walls were 
constructed by two glazing wall leaves of 1.5 m length and 3 m height each, placed in a row. It 
was assumed that the glazing unit that was used for the internal glazing wall construction was 
the commercial single-pane glazing product “Pilkington Optifloat Clear” from Pilkington 
manufacturer [20]. The properties of this glazing product were: glass pane thickness of 12 mm, 
U-value of 5.6 W/m2K, LT of 85%, Solar Transmittance (ST) of 68% and solar heat gain 
coefficient (g) of 74%. These two wall leaves were connected between them without frame. 
However, there was frame in the top and the bottom of these leaves to connected them with the 
ceiling and the floor of the room respectively. In IDA ICE, only glazing units with frame in all 
the four sides can be modeled; not only in the top and the bottom of the glazing unit. As a result, 
it was assumed a quite small frame fraction of 0.05 in order to minimize the effect of the frame 
existence between the two leaves, on the minimum DF inside the meeting room. It was also 
assumed that the frames were constructed based on aluminium and a frame U-value of 2.76 
W/m2K was used [22]. 
In the reference building module of singe man office rooms, the corridor in the middle of 
the building was separated from the two offices by internal wall elements. On the top of these 
internal walls, a glazing unit was placed, in order to provide daylight into the corridor. The 
dimensions of this glazing unit were 3 m length and 1 m height. The glazing unit was placed at 
2 m height in order the top side of the window element to be in aligned with the ceiling. The 
glazing unit used was again the product “Pilkington Optifloat Clear” from Pilkington 
manufacturer [20] of the same properties as the glazing wall leaves of the landscape building 
modules of 15 m and 18 m width. Again, it was assumed for the glazing units a frame of 5 mm 
width, which resulted to a frame fraction of 0.13. The frame it was assumed to be constructed 
from PVC which resulted to a frame U-value of 1.72 W/m2K [22]. 
In addition, as it was described in Chapter 3: Methodology, the different variations of the 
office building system were examined into “sliced” building modules of 3 m façade length. All 
these building modules were created into IDA ICE with adiabatic internal walls on the two 
sides which they were oriented in the East and West direction, as it is illustrated in Figure 4.3.2. 
However, in reality, in the landscape building systems, there are no internal walls separating 
the different open plan areas of the building. IDA ICE did not provide the option to eliminate 
the side internal walls that were not adjusted into the faces of the building envelope. As a result, 
these side internal walls were modeled in order to behave with respect to the daylight 
distribution in the same way as if they were not existed. A solution to this limitation of IDA 
ICE, the inner surfaces of the side internal walls were designed as mirror surfaces, in order to 
reflect the daylight from the window elements on the façade. Through this function, the daylight 
analysis would take into account the daylight level provided into the examined building module, 
by the window elements beyond the side internal walls, that were part of the neighbor identical 
building modules, connected with the examined modules.  
==PAGE:29==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
29 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4.3.2. Illustration from IDA ICE 3-D tab of the side internal wall elements of the landscape building 
module of 13 m (left) and 18 m (middle) building width and all the internal wall elements of the single man offices 
building module of 10 m (right) building width. 
To create these mirror surfaces for the landscape building modules, the longwave emissivity, 
the shortwave reflectance, the roughness and the secularity of the inner surfaces of the side 
internal wall elements were set to 0.9, 1, 0 and 1 respectively. In the rest of the inner surfaces 
of the building modules the longwave emissivity, the roughness and the secularity were set to 
0.9, 0.3 and 0 respectively. Regarding the shortwave reflectance, it was set to 0.7 for the inner 
surfaces of the external walls and the partition walls between the zones, 0.85 for the inner 
surfaces of the internal ceiling and 0.3 for the inner surface of the internal floor. In addition, 
regarding the inner surfaces of the side internal walls of the single man offices building module, 
the same reflectance of the inner partition walls of 0.7 was used. 
The infiltration for each of the five reference modules was set to be 0.3 L/(sm2) at 50 Pa 
pressure difference, distributed proportional to the heated floor area, according to the 
requirements of 2015 Danish Building Regulations of 2020 building energy class [4]. 
4.4. Daylight and lighting 
Regarding the window arrangements of the façades for all the reference building modules, 
the commercial product “Futura+i Tilt-turn window” by IDEALCOMBI A/S [23] was selected, 
as it is illustrated in Figure 4.4.1. This tilt-turn type window was selected because as it is stated 
in the manufacturer’s web page: “The tilt function allows for everyday ventilation whilst the 
turn function is used for cleaning of the outside glass from inside the building” [23].  
 
 
Figure 4.4.1. Schematic of the window element (left) and the sill frame (right) for the “Futura+i Tilt-turn 
window”. The dimensions are in mm [23]. 
This window product was available in a variety of possible combinations regarding the 
window length and the window height. Two tilt-turn window products were selected for each 
of the two façades for all the five-reference building modules. Concerning the height of the 
window elements, it was selected to be 2 m. The windows were placed at 1 m height from the 
floor level in order to provide the possibility of using water radiators as the heating system and 
==PAGE:30==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
30 
 
to provide sufficient outside view to the occupants. Also, the window height of 2 m was selected 
in order to be in align with the height of the room, so as to maximize the daylight level into the 
more centralized areas of the building. Regarding the length of each of the two window units, 
a typical length of 1.3 m was selected in order to provide sufficient daylight level and to 
fulfilling the minimum requirements of the 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4].  
The name of the specific window variation used in the five reference building modules was 
2.6x2 Tilt-turn, and the properties are presented in detail in Appendix A: Window variations 
cases. The rest of the window variation cases which are presented in Appendix A, are part of 
the investigation process that is described in Chapter 5: Daylight and Lighting. 
Regarding the glazing units, a 2-pane and a 3-pane glazing unit were available to be used in 
the “Futura+i Tilt-turn window”. For the five reference building modules, the 3-pane glazing 
unit was selected, due to the improved thermal performance. The total thickness of the glazing 
unit was 48 mm, consisted of three glass panes of 4 mm thickness and two 90% Argon gas 
filled cavities of 18 mm thickness (4-18-4-18-4). According to the Danish energy labelling of 
windows, the performance data of the default glazing unit were: U-value of 0.52 W/m2K, LT 
of 74% and solar heat gain coefficient (g) of 53% [24]. 
According to the product information of the “Futura+i Tilt-turn window” [23], the frame 
width on the façade was 53 mm while the frame depth was 175 mm, as it is illustrated in Figure 
4.4.1. According to the Danish energy labelling of windows, the U-value of the reference 
window element (1.23 m x 1.48 m) was 0.74 W/m2K for a glazing U-value of 0.52 W/m2K 
[24]. However, no data provided for the U-value of the frame element. As a result, the U-value 
of the frame it was calculated based on the equations: 
𝑈𝑤= 𝐴𝑔𝑈𝑔+ 𝐴𝑓𝑈𝑓+ 𝐼𝑔𝛹𝑔
𝐴𝑔+ 𝐴𝑓
 
(1) 
 
 
 𝐴𝑓𝑈𝑓→𝐴𝑓𝑈𝑓+ 𝐼𝑔𝛹𝑔 
(2) 
 
where Uw, Ug, Uf were the heat transfer coefficient (W/m2K) of the reference window element, 
the glazing unit and the frame element respectively, Ag and Af were the areas (m2) on the façade 
of the reference glazing unit and the frame element respectively. Based on the information 
provided by the Danish energy labelling of windows [24], the U-value of the frame element 
was calculated to be 2 W/m2K. 
The luminaries selection was performed in order the minimum requirement of 500 lx in the 
workplaces and meeting room and the requirement of 100 lx in the corridors to be fulfilled, 
according to EN 12464-1 [19]. The type of the luminaries used was high efficient LED 
luminaries from Philips LuxSpace product series [25], [26]. All the LED luminaries were 
selected with dimming function, in order to provide the exact illuminance level required. 
The lighting design with respect to the luminaries’ selection, was performed individually in 
every different zone like the workplace, the corridors and the meeting rooms, of the five 
reference building modules, according to the required illuminance setpoint of 500 lx or 100 lx, 
based on the following equations: 
[required zone illuminance level (lx)]·[zone floor area (m2)]= [required lumens 
(lm) in the zone] 
(3) 
 
 
[nominal lumen output (lm) of one or more identical LED luminaries]≥[required 
lumens (lm) in the zone] 
(4) 
 
 
[required lumens (lm) in the zone]/[total nominal lumen output of all the selected 
luminaries]=[maximum output signal of every luminaire] 
(5) 
 
==PAGE:31==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
31 
 
The LED luminaries were selected in order their nominal lumen output to be exactly the 
same or slightly higher than the required lumen level of the room, either selecting one or more 
luminaries. Through the dimming function of the selected luminaries and the output signal of 
the control strategy of the lighting system, the nominal lumen output was adjusted in order to 
provide the exact requirement of each zone.  
The available input data in IDA ICE for the lighting system for every zone was the number 
of luminaries, the control strategy option, the nominal electrical power per luminaire, the 
luminous efficacy (lm/W) of the identical luminaries and the convective factor, as they are 
illustrated in Figure 4.4.2. 
 
Figure 4.4.2. IDA ICE input data for the lighting design for every zone (workplace, meeting room or corridor). 
The selection of the luminaries for every workplace, corridor and meeting room for all the 
five different building modules is presented in detailed in Appendix C: Luminaries selection.  
Concerning the reference control strategy of the lighting system, a simple ON/OFF strategy 
related to the available daylight was designed. In the ON/OFF control strategy, a simple custom 
Proportional-Integral (PI) controller was created. The input signal of this controller was 
provided by a light sensor placed in every workplace, corridor or meeting room, at the position 
of the occupant. The input signal of this light sensor was the illuminance level (lx) due to the 
daylight inside the zone. The illuminance level due to the available daylight was compared 
through the PI controller with the minimum required illuminance level in the zone, either 500 
lx or 100 lx. If the illuminance level due to daylight was below the set point value, then the 
output digital signal of the controller was set to 1. If the illuminance level was equal or above 
the required illuminance set point then the output digital signal of the PI controller was zero. 
The maximum output signal of the PI controller regarding every workplace, corridor or meeting 
room was selected according to equation 5, in order the maximum luminous flux from the 
luminaries to fulfill exactly the illuminance level. The output digital signal of the PI controller 
was compared with the output digital signal of the operative schedule (1 during the occupancy 
hours or 0 during the rest of the day) and the minimum value between them was the final output 
signal of the control strategy. Through this comparison, the lighting system was always closed 
during the non-occupancy schedule. The operation schedule of the lighting system was from 
08:00 to 17:00 during the weekdays of the year regarding every zone of all the five reference 
building modules. The custom ON/OFF strategy with the PI controller for the illuminance set 
point of 500 lx in IDA ICE is illustrated in Figure 4.4.3. 
==PAGE:32==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy

requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
32 
 
 
Figure 4.4.3. The custom ON/OFF strategy with the PI controller for the illuminance set point of 500 lx in IDA 
ICE. 
The primary energy factor for electricity of 1.8 according to 2015 Danish Building 
Regulations of 2020 building energy class [4], it was used for the calculation of the primary 
energy use for artificial lighting. 
4.5. Heating and cooling 
Concerning the heating and the cooling system of all the five reference building modules, 
ideal heaters and coolers were used in order to detect and fulfill the heating and cooling 
requirements of the building modules. The ideal heaters and coolers were installed in every 
workplace, corridor and meeting room of all the five different reference building modules. 
According to the help section of the IDA ICE software, the ideal heater and cooler are room 
units that heats or cool the zones when no detailed information about an actual room unit, such 
as a radiator or convector, is available or this amount of detail is unmotivated. They have no 
given physical location on any room surface and they are not connected to the plant of the 
building. Physically, they can be considered as standalone fuel or electric heater and cooler 
with fixed performance parameters and no flue gas emissions. 
Regarding the input parameters of the ideal heaters, the maximum power was set to be 2000 
W. This value was selected in order to be higher than the required heat load of each zone in 
order to be able to detect it, because the ideal heater used exactly the capacity it was required 
in every zone regardless the maximum capacity it was set. It was assumed that the ideal heater 
was using district heating to provide space heating, even if it was not connected into the plant, 
in order to use the primary energy factor of district heating for the calculation of primary energy 
use for heating. For the generation efficiency (COP), it was used the default value of 1. Finally, 
the default values of emission efficiency and long wave radiation fraction or 1 and 0.4 were 
used respectively. The primary energy factor for the calculation of the primary energy use for 
heating based on district heating was 0.6, according to 2015 Danish Building Regulations of 
2020 building energy class [4].The input parameters of the ideal heater used in IDA ICE are 
illustrated in Figure 4.5.1. 
Regarding the input parameters of the ideal cooler, the maximum power was also set to 2000 
W. As the ideal heater, the ideal cooler used exactly the capacity it was required in every zone 
regardless the maximum capacity it was set. It was assumed that the ideal cooler was an electric 
based device. The default cooling coil temperature of 15oC it was used. The default value 
regarding the coefficient of performance (COP) for the refrigeration cycle of 3 it was also used 
for the ideal cooler. Finally, the default value of 1 regarding the emission efficiency was used. 
The primary energy factor for the calculation of the primary energy use for cooling based on 
electric cooling was 1.8, according to 2015 Danish Building Regulations of 2020 building 
energy class [4]. The input parameters of the ideal cooler used in IDA ICE are illustrated in 
Figure 4.5.1. 
==PAGE:33==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
33 
 
 
Figure 4.5.1. Input parameters regarding the ideal heater and the ideal cooler used in every zone of the five-
reference building system in IDA ICE. 
For the operation of both the ideal heaters and coolers, a new custom control strategy was 
created, based once again on a PI controller. In this control strategy, the operative temperature 
in every zone of the five reference building modules was detected by a temperature sensor 
placed in the position of the occupant. This sensor was connected with the PI controller. The 
input signal of the operative temperature into the PI controller it was compared with the heating 
set point for the ideal heaters and the cooling set point for the ideal cooler. According to EN 
15251 [5], the heating set point was set to 20oC and the cooling set point was set to 26oC. In the 
case of the ideal heater, as long as the operative temperature input signal was equal or above 
the heating set point then the ideal heater was deactivated. In the case of ideal cooler, as long 
as the operative temperature input signal was equal or above the cooling set point then the ideal 
heater was activated. Then the output digital signal (0 or 1) of the PI controller for both ideal 
heater and cooler was compared with the digital output signal of the operative schedule (1 
during occupancy or 0 during the non-occupancy hours of the day) and the minimum value 
between them was the final output signal of the control strategy.  
It was decided that the ideal heaters and coolers to operate during all the hours of every day 
of the year. This decision was made in order to minimize the capacity and as a result the initial 
cost of the heating and cooling system. If the operative schedule of the heating or cooling 
system was adjusted into the occupancy schedule, the preheating or precooling operation of the 
ideal heaters and coolers in the morning hours could increase the required capacity of each 
system for a quite short period of use. The control strategy for the operation of the ideal heaters 
and coolers is illustrating in Figure 4.5.2. 
 
Figure 4.5.2. The control strategy for the operation of the ideal heaters and coolers in IDA ICE 
Regarding the internal gains, one occupant was placed in every workplace of the five 
reference building modules. The activity level was adjusted to represent a sedentary activity of 
1.2 MET. The clothing insulation was set to be 0.85±0.25 and it was automatically adjusted 
==PAGE:34==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
34 
 
between the limits in order to obtain comfort.  The occupancy schedule was selected to be from 
08:00 to 17:00 every week day of the year. Regarding the meeting rooms in the relevant 
reference building modules, it was assumed that in design conditions they were not occupied 
during all the working hours, because the permanent occupants of the office building system 
were placed into their workplaces and no additional occupants were in the building.  
It has to be mentioned that the workplaces in each of the four landscape building modules, 
were designed like task areas in order to perform the lighting design. The real working area of 
the occupants was the total open plan area of each of the building module, taking into account 
the corridors between the task areas. As a result, in Table 4.5.1, the working area per occupant 
is illustrated for the five different building modules. In the landscape building modules with 
meeting room in the middle, the meeting room area was excluded from the calculation of the 
working area per occupant.

In addition, one laptop computer was included as equipment in every workplace of the five 
reference building modules. According to 2013 ASHRAE Handbook – Fundamentals (SI) [27], 
it was decided the laptop computer to be similar to “Manufacturer 1; 2.0 GHz processor, 2 GB 
RAM, 355 mm screen” with average power at 31 W. The operating schedule of the equipment 
in every workplace was selected to be identical to the occupancy schedule. The primary energy 
use for electricity due to the equipment operation was not taken into account for the overall 
primary energy use of the office building system according to 2015 Danish Building 
Regulations of 2020 building energy class [4]. 
Also, the heat gains due to the operation of the lighting system in every zone of the five 
reference building modules were considered internal gains. All the electrical input to the 
luminaries ends up as heat inside the zone because the light is absorbed by the surfaces and 
become heat. 
In order to reduce the energy requirements for cooling into the five-reference building 
modules, a passive cooling strategy was selected. The passive cooling strategy used in order to 
reduce the solar gains through the window elements was the implementation of external blinds. 
However, the implementation of external blinds, except the reduction of the solar gains through 
the windows, could reduce the available daylight level inside the building modules and as a 
result to increase the primary energy use for artificial lighting. 
The default option of the external blinds from the library of IDA ICE was selected in every 
window unit of the five reference building modules. The shading factor regarding the LT of the 
combined glazing unit and shading element was selected to be 0.09, while regarding the solar 
heat gain coefficient (g) was selected to be 0.14. The operation of the external blinds did not 
affect the U-value (W/m2K) of the glazing unit, as it is illustrated in Figure 4.5.3. 
==PAGE:35==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
35 
 
 
Figure 4.5.3. The shading coefficients regarding the solar heat gain coefficient (g), the LT and the U-value of 
the External blinds shading device, as they are illustrated in IDA ICE. 
Regarding the control strategy of the external blinds, the default control strategy of IDA ICE 
was used. Based on this strategy, the solar irradiation was measured at the outer side of the 
window element. If the solar irradiation level was higher than 100 W/m2, the external blinds 
were drawn. In addition, if the solar irradiation incident angle was below 90o, the external blinds 
were also drawn, regardless the irradiation level measured. The operation of the external blinds 
was during both the occupied and unoccupied hours of the day. 
Except from the external blinds, another important passive cooling strategy to reduce the 
operative temperature of the zones and the primary energy use for cooling was the natural 
venting through opening the windows. However, in an office building system the occupant’s 
satisfaction with the working environment is quite important in order to increase their 
productivity. Even if the natural venting through the windows could improve their thermal 
comfort, on the other hand, the acoustic environment could be compromised. In most of the 
cases, the office building systems are located close to crowded roads or boulevards and by 
opening the window elements the noise level could increase the occupant’s dissatisfaction with 
their working environment. As a result, it was decided not to use natural venting through the 
windows in order to improve the thermal comfort of the occupants and reduce the primary 
energy use for cooling. 
4.6. Ventilation 
The ventilation system used for every zone of the five reference building systems was based 
on a Constant Air Volume (CAV) strategy. According to EN 15251 for the Category II of indoor 
environment [5], for low polluting building, the ventilation rate qtot (l/s) for a zone is calculated 
from the equation: 
𝑞𝑡𝑜𝑡= 𝑁∙7 𝑙𝑠∙𝑜𝑐𝑐
⁄
+ 𝐴∙0.7 𝑙𝑠∙𝑚2
⁄
 
(6) 
 
where N is the number of occupants and A is the floor area (m2) of the room. The ventilation 
system was set to operate during both the occupied and unoccupied hours of the day, in order 
to remove the contaminants due to the materials of the building envelop.  
In IDA ICE, the ventilation system and as a result the ventilation rates should be set 
regarding every zone created, like the workplaces, the corridors and the meeting rooms, 
according to the maximum and minimum required ventilation flows (l/sm2). Taking into 
account that no occupants were placed in the corridor zones of the building modules in IDA 
ICE, the calculation of the ventilation flow rates based on the equation 6, would resulted into 
high values in the workplaces and the minimum value in the corridor, which it was not realistic 
taking into account that the workplaces and the corridors in the landscape building modules 
were considered open plan areas. In an open plan area, there should be a universal ventilation 
rate.  
==PAGE:36==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
36 
 
To overcome this limitation, the ventilation flow rates were calculated for the workplaces 
and corridors based on a different number of occupants for every zone than what it was set in 
the IDA ICE modes. The occupants of the open plan areas were “divided” equally into the 
workplaces and the corridors in the four landscape building modules. Taking into account the 
share of the total open plan area related to the workplaces and corridors, the total number of 
occupants in the open plan area, the number of the occupants in every zone for the calculation 
only of the ventilation flow rates is presented in Table 4.6.1. 
In Table 4.6.2, the ventilation rates (in l/sm2 and in l/s) for all the zones of the five reference 
building modules during the occupancy schedule of the day are presented. The calculation of 
the maximum ventilation flow rates was performed based on the equation 6, taking into account 
as the number of the occupants in each zone (A) the values of Table 4.6.1 and as the floor area 
of each zone the values from Table 4.2.1. Regarding the minimum ventilation rate during the 
non-occupancy hours of the day, it was set to be 0.7 l/sm2 regarding every zone of the five 
reference building modules due to zero occupants.

In order to implement this CAV ventilation strategy of the different ventilation rates during 
the occupied and unoccupied hours, a custom control strategy was created based only on a 
schedule. The digital output signal of the ventilation schedule during the occupied hours from 
08:00 to 17:00 was set to 1, while for the rest of the day it was set to zero. When the output 
digital signal of the ventilation system was 1, the maximum ventilation air flow rates were 
provided in the different zones and when the output digital signal was 0, the minimum 
ventilation rates were provided. The CAV ventilation strategy for the different supply air flow 
rates during the day is illustrated in Figure 4.6.2. 
 
Figure 4.6.2. The CAV ventilation strategy for the different supply air flow rated during the day, as it is 
illustrated in IDA ICE. 
The standard air handling unit (AHU) of IDA ICE was used and it is illustrated in Figure 
4.6.3. This AHU was consisted of one fan element on the supply side, one fan element on the 
return side, a heat exchanger between the supply and the return side and one heating and cooling 
coil on the supply side. 
==PAGE:38==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
38 
 
 
Figure 4.6.3. The air handling unit (AHU) used in IDA ICE. 
The two coils in the supply side were deactivated completely. The supply temperature of the 
AHU was set to a constant minimum value of 18oC during all the year period. Responsible to 
maintain this minimum supply temperature was the heat exchanger of the system. The 
efficiency of the heat exchanger was set to 85% and the operation schedule was set for all the 
hours of the day. The fan operation schedule was the operation schedule of the ventilation 
system and it was set to be activated during all the hours of all the day. According to 2015 
Danish Building Regulations of 2020 building energy class: “For ventilations systems for multi-
storey buildings, the specific power consumption for air movement must not exceed 1200 J/m3 
fresh air in the background air change” [4]. As a result, the specific fan power (SFP) of both of 
the fans was set to 1200 J/m3, equally divided at 600 J/m3 between the supply and the return 
fan. 
4.7. Domestic Hot Water (DHW) use 
 According to the SBI Direction 213 [28], the domestic hot water (DHW) use for a typical 
office building was set to 100 l/m2 of floor area during a year period. This DHW use it was 
distributed into the occupancy schedule of the building system, from 08:00 to 17:00 during the 
weekdays. 
The source of the DHW of the office building system it was assumed to be the district 
heating network. The supply temperature of the DHW it was set to 55oC. The temperature of 
the cold water according to SBI Direction 213 should be 10oC. However, the default 
temperature of the cold water in IDA ICE it was set to be 5oC without changing possibility. As 
a result, in order to use the correct temperature difference for the calculation of the primary 
energy use for DHW, the supply temperature of the DHW it was set to 50oC. The primary 
energy factor used in order to calculate the primary energy use for DHW it was the primary 
energy factor for district heating of 0.6, according to 2015 Danish Building Regulations of 2020 
building energy class [4]. 
4.8. Indoor climate and primary energy use investigation conditions 
All the five different reference building modules were investigated regarding the design heat 
load requirements, the minimum Daylight Factor (DF) in the permanent workplaces, the indoor 
climate conditions regarding the thermal comfort and the indoor air quality and the overall 
primary energy use for artificial lighting, heating, cooling, DHW and ventilation. 
Regarding the design heat load calculations, the outdoor conditions of -12oC of dry-bulb 
temperature with zero solar irradiation and no internal gains were considered under static 
==PAGE:39==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
39 
 
approach, based on DS 418 [29]. The IDA ICE simulations for the calculation of the minimum 
DF, in every permanent workplace of each of the five reference building modules, were 
performed under CIE Overcast sky conditions with high precision and the external blinds never 
drawn. The minimum DF was measured at horizontal surfaces inside every zone, at height 
above floor of 0.8 m, distance from the internal surface of the walls of 0.5 m and resolution of 
0.5 m. The location of the examined reference building modules used in every investigation 
performed in IDA ICE was the area of Copenhagen/Kastrup with a Latitude of 55.62 N, a 
Longitude of 12.65 E and an elevation of 5 m. The climate file used for the simulations in IDA 
ICE was based on the Danish Reference Year (DRY) of 2013. 
4.9. Indoor climate and primary energy use results

presented. According to the results, the design heat load of the landscape boiling modules of 
13, 15 and 18 m building width resulted into the same design heat load due to the same floor 
area of 5.4 m2 of the workplaces. In the landscape building module of 10 m width, the design 
heat load was lower due to the higher floor area of 6 m2 of the workplaces. Also, the single man 
office reference building module resulted to less than the half of the design heat load of the 
landscape office of the same building width of 10 m, due to the internal wall elements that 
separated the central corridor for the single man offices and course the double floor area of 12 
m2 of the office room.

The results regarding the thermal comfort and the indoor air quality were fully complied 
with the recommendations of the EN 15251 [5] and ISO 7730 [30]. The reason was that due to 
the operation of ideal heaters and coolers combined with the PI controller, the operating 
temperature was never outside of the acceptable range from the heating set point to the cooling 
set point temperature, regardless the energy required in order the operative temperature to be 
maintained between the two set points.  
The operative temperature in the landscape building modules of higher number of 
workplaces was fluctuating in slightly higher temperature range during most of the year period.

module of 10 m width are illustrated. The first week was from 23rd of January to 29th of January, 
the second week was from 10th of April to 16th of April and the last week was from 3rd of July 
to 9th of July.  
Regarding the indoor air quality based on the CO2 concentration, the results were following 
the same trend due to the same occupancy schedule for every day of the year. In Figure 4.9.4, 
the CO2 concentration (ppm) of the South workplace 1 of the same building module was 
illustrated for a random day of the year on 25th of April. According to the results, the CO2 
concentration was fluctuating close to 850 ppm during the occupancy hours of the day.

According to Table 4.9.3, none of the reference building modules was in compliance with 
the maximum overall primary energy performance requirement of 25 W/m2K for non-
residential buildings, as it was defined in  2015 Danish Building Regulations of 2020 building 
energy class [4]. The primary energy use for artificial lighting and for ventilation was quite 
high. On the other hand, the energy use for heating and cooling was quite low.  
In the landscape building module of 13 m and 18 m width, the primary energy use for heating 
was lower due to the increased internal gains related to the higher number of occupants, 
comparing to landscape building variation of 10 m and 15 m width respectively. However, as 
the building width was increasing the primary energy use for heating was decreasing due to the 
reduced ratio of the transmission heat losses from the façade elements to the total heated floor 
area of the building modules.  
In addition, the primary energy use for heating in the landscape building modules with a 
meeting room in the middle (15 m and 18 m) comparing to the landscape building modules 
without meeting room (10 m and 13 m), was lower due the presence of the internal glazing wall 
elements. The heat transfer through the glazing elements was lower comparing to the modules 
of no internal glazing elements. The lower heat transfer resulted into reduced requirements for 
primary energy use for heating in the meeting rooms. Taking also into account the increased 
==PAGE:42==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
42 
 
floor area of the building modules with the meeting room in the center, the result was the 
decreased energy use for heating and cooling. 
Regarding the primary energy for cooling, as the building width was increasing the required 
energy for cooling was reduced due to the reduced effect of the solar gains in the more 
centralized areas of the building module, even if the internal gains were higher from the 
landscape building module of 10 m to 13 m width due to the higher number of workplaces. 
Also, the existence of the internal glazing walls in the meeting rooms, reduced the solar gains 
in the meeting room and as a result the energy use for cooling. However, in the landscape 
building module of 18 m width the primary energy use for cooling was slightly higher than the 
landscape building module of 15 m width, due to the increased internal gains related to the 
number of the occupants. In the wider building modules of 15 m and 18 m, the effect of the 
internal gains was become more significant than the effect of the solar gains through the façade 
windows. 
Concerning the single man offices building module, the primary energy use for heating was 
higher comparing to the landscape building module of the same width of 10 m, due to the lower 
internal gains related to the lower number of occupants. The lower internal gains were also the 
reason of the reduced primary energy for cooling.  
The primary energy use for ventilation for the landscape building modules of 15 m and 18 
m width was lower than the landscape building modules of 10 m and 13 m respectively due to 
the presence of the unoccupied meeting rooms. The maximum ventilation flow rate in the 
meeting rooms was always related only to the building emissions and taking into account the 
increased floor area, the primary energy use for ventilation was reduced. However, comparing 
the landscape building modules of two workplaces with the building modules of three 
workplaces close to the facades, the primary energy use for ventilation was higher due to the 
increased number of occupants and the reduced working area per occupant, which they were 
resulted into higher total ventilation flor rates (l/s), according to Table 4.6.2.  
Although, the most critical part of the overall primary energy use of the building modules 
was the primary energy use for artificial lighting. Due to the inefficient operation control of the 
lighting system, the differences in the available daylight regarding the building modules of 
different width were not reflected into the reduction of the primary energy use for lighting for 
the building modules of lower width. As a result, there was a significant waste of energy due 
to the unnecessary use of the lighting system. More specifically, the primary energy use for 
artificial lighting was increasing along with the increase of the building width. Finally, the 
primary energy use for lighting in the single man offices was higher comparing to the landscape 
building module of 10 m width, due to the reduced daylight level affected by the lower 
reflectance of the side walls and the existence of the partition walls between the corridor and 
the office rooms that minimized the available daylight in the corridor and the daylight 
interaction from the two opposite façades. 
In order to reduce the energy use for lighting, as it was described in Chapter 3: Methodology, 
an investigation process was performed regarding a more efficient lighting control strategy, 
different window variation cases regarding the size of the glazing area and a lower illuminance 
set point. This investigation procedure is presented in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting. In 
addition, the implementation of a thinner façade element was investigated regarding the 
increase of the available daylight and the results are presented in Chapter 6: Improved façade 
element. The results regarding the primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling based 
on the shading operation and the use of solar control glazing units were in Chapter 7: Primary 
energy use. Concerning the reduction of the primary energy for ventilation, as it was described 
also in Chapter 3: Methodology, a new ventilation strategy based on Demand Controlled 
Ventilation (DCV) principles was also investigated and is presented in Chapter 7: Primary 
energy use. In addition, in Chapter 8: Sensitivity analysis, the number of occupants was reduced 
in order to identify the reduction mainly in the primary energy use for ventilation but also the 
effect on the primary energy use for heating and cooling due to the reduced internal gains.

Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting 
5.1. Introduction 
In this Chapter, the daylight analysis and the artificial lighting design, as it was described in 
Chapter 3: Methodology, are presented analytically. In this part of the investigation process, 
different variations of every of the five reference office building modules were created in IDA 
ICE. The parameters that were investigated in the different variation models were the glazing 
area, the type of the window elements used, with respect to the frame area, and the Light 
Transmittance (LT) value of the glazing units. A daylight analysis was performed and the 
results were expressed by the minimum Daylight Factor (DF) (%) in every permanent 
workplace, for every different window variation case created of the five reference building 
modules. 
According to the minimum DF results, some variations were rejected because the minimum 
DF in the permanent workplaces was not fulfilling the minimum requirement of 2% according 
to the 2015 Danish Building Regulations [4]. In the acceptable variation cases based on the 
minimum DF results, the artificial lighting design was performed. In the lighting design part of 
the investigation process, the number and the properties of the luminaries used were selected 
in order to fulfill the illuminance requirements, according to EN 12646-1 [19]. Regarding the 
control of the lighting system, a new control strategy was designed in order to provide 
supplementary artificial light to the available daylight. All the accepted window variation cases 
according to the DF results were investigated regarding the primary energy use for artificial 
lighting based on the new lighting control strategy, but for two different illuminance set point 
values. The calculation of the primary energy use for artificial lighting was performed through 
dynamic simulations in IDA ICE software. 
The external blinds of the reference building modules were deactivated in all the different 
window variation cases, in order to identify the effect only due the new control strategy and the 
different illuminance set point related to the primary energy use for lighting. 
5.2. Description of the variation models for the daylight analysis 
In the beginning of the investigation procedure, all the possible limitations regarding the 
façade windows design were identified. On both façades of the five building modules, in most 
of the variation cases investigated, more than one identical window units were used, creating 
window arrangements. The maximum window arrangement length, was the maximum available 
façade length of 3 m. The window arrangement length was reducing by 0.2 m in every different 
window variation case. The minimum length of the window arrangements was decided based 
on the daylight investigation. According to the requirements of the 2015 Danish Building 
Regulations [4], there must be a minimum 2% DF in every permanent workplace. As a result, 
as long as the minimum DF in all the workplaces of every window variation was higher than 
2%, based on IDA ICE simulations, the length of the windows was further reduced and 
minimum DF was investigated.  
The height of the window units it was selected to be 2 m. This choice was based on three 
specific requirements. The first was that outside view should be provided to the seated 
occupants in order to feel satisfied and comfortable with their working environment. The 
second requirement was that water radiators could be placed below the windows as a heating 
system, in order to eliminate the draught rate. As a result, the window elements were places at 
1 m height from the floor level, in order both of the first two requirements to be fulfilled. The 
third requirement was that the window elements should be in aligned with the ceiling height of 
the room, in order to maximize the DF in the more centralized areas of the building. As a result, 
taking into account the 1 m height of the façade wall below the windows and the room height 
of 3 m for every of the five reference building modules, the height of the window units was 
selected to be 2 m. 
Regarding the type of the windows used, the commercial “Futura+i Tilt-turn window” 
product by IDEALCOMBI A/S [23] which it was introduced in Chapter 4: Reference building 
==PAGE:44==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
44 
 
system was used in different available sizes. According to the manufacturers datasheet, the 
maximum available product length was 1.3 m for 2 m height, as it is illustrated in Figure 5.2.1. 
Based on Figure 5.2.1, in order to create the window arrangement variation of 3 m length, three 
identical window units were used of 1m length each, due to the length limitation of the product. 
In the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m length, three window units were used also, but 
not of the same length. The length of the middle window unit was 1 m and the length of the two 
side window units was 0.9 m. In the rest of the window variations cases that the tilt-turn window 
type was used, two identical window units placed in the middle of the façade were selected with 
the appropriate length and always 2 m height. 
In addition, window variation cases were examined for fixed window type units, due to the 
higher available length for a single window unit, that could reduce the window frame area on 
the façade and increase the available daylight through the windows. The fixed window product 
selected was the commercial “Futura+i Fixed light window”, also from IDEALCOMBI A/S 
[31]. The design of this window was identical to the tilt-turn version, except that it was a fixed 
window unit without opening ability and the range of available products sizes was quite higher. 
The frame width on the façade of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” was the same as in the tilt-
turn version, at 53 mm. In Figure 5.2.1, the range of the available window sizes of the “Futura+i 
Fixed light window” are illustrated. In Figure 5.2.2, the schematic of the window element and 
the sill frame of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” are illustrated. 
 
Figure 5.2.1. The range of the available window sizes of the “Futura+i Tilt-turn window” (left) [23]. The 
range of the available window sizes of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” [31] (right). 
As in can be seen in Figure 5.2.1, the maximum available product length of the “Futura+i 
Fixed light window” was 2.4 m at 2 m window height. As a result, in the window arrangement 
variation of 3 m length, two identical window units of 1.5 m length were used in both façades. 
Also, in the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m length, two identical window units of 1.4 
m length each were used. In the window arrangement variation of 2.6 m length, there was also 
no available single window unit at 2 m height. As a result, two identical window units of 1.3 m 
length each were selected. Regarding the rest of the windows arrangement variation cases, 
starting from 2.4 m length and lower, single window units were available and they were used.

exactly the same. The only difference between the two different window types was the opening 
possibility of the tilt-turn window type. Based on this fact, these two cases were examined as 
one variation case.

Graph 5.2.1. The window area (m2), the glazing area (m2) and the frame area (m2) on each of the two facades

The glazing unit of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” was the same default glazing unit of 
the “Futura+i Tilt-turn window”, as it was presented in Chapter 4: Reference building system. 
However, this default glazing unit of three glass panes of 4 mm thickness and two 90% Argon 
filled cavities of 18 mm thickness was not appropriate to be used in all the different window 
variation cases. According to GLASFAKTA 2015 product catalog from Pilkington [20], for a 
constant window height of 2 m, the thickness of the first glass pane should be increased from 4 
mm to 6 mm, for all window units with length higher than 1.8 m in order to be able to resist in 
the pressure forces due to the wind. In the variation cases that the tilt-turn window unit was 
used, there was no available window size more than 1.8 m length, according to Figure 5.2.1. 
As a result, the default glazing properties of 4-18-4-18-4 of “Futura+i Tilt-turn window” [23] 
were used. For the window variation cases that the fixed window type was used, there were 
arrangements of single window units of more than 1.8 m length. These cases were the window 
arrangement variation of 2.4 m, 2.2 m, 2 m and 1.8 m length respectively. In these cases, a 
special enhanced construction of the “Futura+i Fixed light” glazing unit was created. 
However, there were no available data by IDEALCOMBI A/S related to the characteristics 
of the glass panes used for the construction of the glazing unit of the default Futura+i window 
products. The only available data provided were that the default construction of the 3-pane 
glazing unit consisted of three glass panes of 4 mm thickness each and two cavities of 18 mm 
thickness between them, each filled with Argon gas at 90% [23],[31]. In addition, the 
performance data of the combined default glazing unit, provided by the Danish energy labelling 
of windows [24], as it was described in Chapter 4: Reference building system. Based on these 
performance data, the number and the thicknesses of the glass panes and the cavities, an 
identical to the default glazing unit of the Futura+i window products was selected from 
GLASFAKTA 2015 product catalog [20]. This glazing unit was the 4S(3)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4 
of “Energiglas” category, constructed by two Pilkington Optitherm S3 glazing panes of 4 mm 
thickness in the two sides and a Pilkington Optifloat Clear pane of 4 mm thickness in the 
middle. The two cavities between the three glass panes were identical to the default construction 
of the Futura+i window product. The properties of the selected glazing unit were U-value of 
0.5 W/m2K, LT of 71% and solar heat gain coefficient (g) of 50%, close enough to the properties 
of the default Futura+i glazing unit of 0.52 W/m2K U-value, 74% LT and solar heat gain 
coefficient (g) of 53% [24].  
This selected glazing unit from GLASFAKTA 2015 product catalog was created into the 
Spectrum online software by Pilkington [32] and the outer Pilkington Optitherm S3 pane of 4 
mm thickness was replaced by a pane of the same type but 6 mm thickness. The new glazing 
unit created was the 6S(3)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4, the U-value was 0.55 W/m2K, the LT was 74%

and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 51%. This special construction of the enhanced

the characteristics of this special enhanced glazing unit of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” 
made by Pilkington are presented in detail in Appendix B: Glazing units. 
In addition, the window arrangement variation of 3 m length was designed in order a high 
insulated 4-pane glazing unit to be used. This 4-pane glazing unit was used for both the tilt-turn 
and fixed type of the Futura+i window unit. This new 4-pane glazing unit was created by 
Pilkington glass panes in Spectrum online software. Based on the characteristics of the default 
glazing unit of the Futura+i window products with three panes of 4 mm thickness and two 
cavities filled with Argon (90%) gas of 18 mm thickness, one more glass pane of 4 mm 
thickness was added after a third cavity filled with Argon (90%) gas and thickness of 18 mm. 
As a result, the glazing properties of the 4-pane window unit were 4-18-4-18-4-18-4 and the 
total thickness of the combined glazing unit was 70 mm. The glass panes by Pilkington used in 
Spectrum to create the 4-pane glazing unit. Three Pilkington Optitherm S3 OW panes were 
used in the positions 1, 3 and 4 from the external to the internal side of the glazing unit and a 
Pilkington Optifloat Clear pane in the position 2. However, the default frame construction of 
the Futura+i window products was available only for glazing units up to 48 mm thickness. Due 
to the total thickness of 70 mm of the 4-pane glazing unit, it was assumed that the maximum 
acceptable glazing thickness in the frame construction of the Futura+i window products could 
be extended up to 70 mm, constructed in a way that the U-value of the frame element to remain 
2 W/m2K. The 4-pane version of the Futura+i glazing unit made by Pilkington products was 
the 4wS(3)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4w-18Ar-S(3)4w. The U-value was 0.36 W/m2K, the LT was 
70% and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 49%. The properties and the characteristics of 
this 4-pane special glazing unit of the Futura+i window products made by Pilkington are 
presented in detail in Appendix B: Glazing units. 
Furthermore, the possibility of using solar control coating glazing units for the Futura+i 
window products, as a solution to reduce the solar gains, was examined. However, the use of 
solar control coating glazing units reduces the LT value of the glazing. When the LT value is 
reduced, the available daylight is reduced and the energy requirements for artificial lighting are 
higher. As a result, the relation between the use of solar control coating glazing units and the 
minimum DF in the permanent workplaces was also investigated in this paragraph. Later, in the 
artificial lighting design, the relation between the use of solar control coating glazing units and 
the primary energy use for lighting was investigated. 
However, the default selected Futura+i window product did not provide the possibility to 
implement solar control coating. To overcome this limitation, a special construction of the 
Futura+i glazing unit with solar control coating was created by Pilkington glazing products. 
Once again, the identical to the default glazing unit made by Pilkington products 4S(3)-18Ar-
4-18Ar-S(3)4 was used as a reference case in Spectrum online software [32]. In this special 
construction of the glazing unit, it was assumed that the thicknesses of all the glass panes and 
cavities would remain the same in order the U-value of the glazing unit no to be changed 
significantly. The outer pane was replaced by a Pilkington Suncool 70/35 OW pane. The name 
of the new glazing unit for the Futura+i window products with solar control coating was 
4wC(70)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4. The U-value was 0.5 W/m2K, the LT was 66% and the solar heat 
gain coefficient (g) was 36%. The properties and the characteristics of this special glazing unit 
with solar control coating of the Futura+i window products made by Pilkington are presented 
in detail in Appendix B: Glazing units. 
Furthermore, a glazing unit with solar control coating was used for the cases of single 
window unit with more than 1.8 m length. Once again, a new enhanced glazing unit with solar 
control coating was created for the “Futura+i Fixed light window”. The previously created 
enhanced glazing unit with no solar control coating 6S(3)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4 was inserted in 
Spectrum online software [32] and the Pilkington Optitherm S3 glazing pane of 6 mm thickness 
was replaced by a Pilkington Suncool 70/35 OW of also 6 mm thickness. The new glazing unit

48 
 
created was the 6wC(70)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4. The U-value was 0.53 W/m2K, the LT was 66% 
and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 36%. This special enhanced glazing unit with solar 
control coating made by Pilkington products for the “Futura+i Fixed light window”, was used 
for the window arrangement variation of 2.4 m, 2.2 m, 2 m and 1.8 m length respectively. The 
properties and the characteristics of this special enhanced glazing unit with solar control coating 
of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” made by Pilkington are presented in detail in Appendix 
B: Glazing units. 
Finally, for the window arrangement variations of 3 m length, the use of solar control glazing 
units was investigated also. The created 4-pane special glazing unit of the Futura+i window 
4wS(3)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4w-18Ar-S(3)4w, for both tilt-turn and fixed window types was 
inserted into Spectrum online software [32] and the outer Pilkington Optitherm S3 OW pane of 
4 mm thickness was replaced by a Pilkington Suncool 70/35 OW pane of 4 mm thickness. The 
new glazing unit created was the 4wC(70)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4w-18Ar-S(3)4w. The U-value 
was 0.35 W/m2K, the LT was 61% and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 34%. The 
properties and the characteristics of this 4-pane special glazing unit with solar control coating 
of the Futura+i window products made by Pilkington are presented in detail in Appendix B: 
Glazing units. 
All the different window variation cases of the tilt-turn and fixed window types and glazing 
units with or without solar control coating based on Futura+i window product were investigated 
for all the five different reference building modules. 
The investigation of the minimum DF (%) was based on IDA ICE simulations. The 
investigation started from the window arrangements of higher length to the arrangements of 
lower length, for the two window types and the glazing with or without solar control coating, 
until the minimum DF of 2% in every permanent workplace, based on the 2015 Danish Building 
Regulations [4], not to be fulfilled. 
The IDA ICE simulations for the calculation of the minimum DF, in every permanent 
workplace-zone of each of the five reference building modules, as it was also described in 
Chapter 4: Reference building system, were performed under CIE Overcast sky conditions with 
high precision and the shading device never drawn. The minimum DF was measured at 
horizontal surfaces inside every zone, at height above floor of 0.8 m, distance from the internal 
surface of the walls of 0.5 m and resolution of 0.5 m.  
5.3. Daylight analysis results 
The daylight analysis results were expressed by the minimum DF (%) in every permanent 
workplace, in all the five different reference building modules. An acceptable window variation 
was identified by a green marked cell, while a rejected variation was identified by a red marked 
cell. The critical workplace in all the cases was the North workplace 2 for the landscape building 
modules of two workplaces close to each façade and the North workplace 3 for the landscape 
building modules of three workplaces close to each façade. These zones were the most 
centralized permanent workplaces of the North façade. In the single man offices building 
module the critical zone was the North office. In all the window variation cases examined, the 
minimum DF result were higher in the cases that the fixed type window elements were used for 
the same window arrangement length, due to the higher glazing area, as it was presented in 
Table 5.2.1 and illustrated in Graph 5.2.1. 
In Table 5.3.1 and Table 5.3.2, the daylight results regarding the reference building module 
of 10 m building width are presented.

According to the results, for the glazing units without solar control coating, the highest value 
regarding the minimum DF of 3.3% it was noticed in the window arrangement variation of 2.8 
m length for the fixed window type. The lower acceptable window arrangement variation of 
tilt-turn window type was for 1.8 m length and minimum DF of 2% in the North office. The 
lower acceptable window arrangement variation of fixed window type was for 1.6 m length and 
minimum DF of 2% in the North office.  
In the variations of glazing units with solar control coating, the highest value regarding the 
minimum DF of 2.8% it was noticed once again in the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m 
length for the fixed window type. The lower acceptable window arrangement variation of tilt-
turn window type was for 2.2 m length and minimum DF of 2.1% in the North office. The lower 
acceptable window arrangement variation of fixed window type was for 1.8 m length and 
minimum DF of 2% in the North office. 
According to the overall results for all the five different building modules, it was observed 
that the landscape building module of 10 m building width resulted into higher values for the 
minimum DF in the critical zone as it was expected. The results for the single man office 
building module of the same building width were lower due to the different reflectance used in 
the inner surfaces of the internal walls. In the landscape building module of 10 m, a reflectance 
of 1 it was used to eliminate the effect of the internal wall presence to the light distribution in 
the building model created in IDA ICE, comparing to the real conditions of the light distribution 
with no internal wall presence. Also, the presence of internal wall between the office rooms 
and the corridor in the middle, reduced the interaction of the light entered from the two opposite 
façades.  
For the rest of the landscape building modules, for the building modules of three workplaces 
close to each façade of the building, the minimum DF results were significantly lower in the 
critical zone due to the higher distance of the zone from the façade, comparing to the building 
modules of two workplaces close to each façade. 
Finally, in the comparison between the building modules of the same workplaces close to 
the facades, it was observed that the increased building with resulted into lower minimum DF 
results for the critical zone, as it was expected. 
5.4. Description of the variation models for the artificial lighting design 
After the calculation procedure through IDA ICE simulations to identify the minimum DF 
(%) in every permanent workplace for the five building modules and for every different window 
variation case, the investigation process was focused on the artificial lighting design and the 
energy calculations in order to identify the primary energy use for artificial lighting. The 
==PAGE:55==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
55 
 
primary energy use for lighting was the output of the energy used by the lighting system, 
multiplied by the primary energy factor for electricity of 1.8, according to the 2015 Danish 
Building Regulations for the energy class of 2020 [4]. 
As it was briefly described in Chapter 3: Methodology, all the window variations of the five 
reference building modules, for the glazing units with and without solar control coating, that 
were accepted regarding the minimum DF results, they were investigated regarding the artificial 
lighting design and primary energy use for lighting. The variation parameter of the investigation 
was the required illuminance set point for the operation of the lighting system in the permanent 
workplaces and meeting rooms.  
Regarding the required illuminance level, two different set point values for the workplaces 
and meeting rooms were used: 500 lx according to EN 12464-1 [19] and 300 lx. The second set 
point value of 300 lx was selected in order to evaluate the reduction of the primary energy use 
for artificial lighting due to the use of lower illuminance set point. The use of a lower 
illuminance set point can be justified by the supplementary lighting provided from the computer 
and laptops screens. Nowadays, most of the office work is performed through computer devices 
that provide the necessary illuminance level in order to operate them. Also, according to EN 
12464-1 [19] the illuminance set point of 500 lx is related mostly with paper work which is 
limited due to the use of the computer devices. Also, the supplementary artificial light from the 
computer devices could reduce the necessity of higher illuminance level from the lighting 
system in every workplace. The illuminance set point for the corridors was always 100 lx, 
according to EN 12464-1 [19]. 
The luminaries’ selection for the five reference building modules regarding the illuminance 
set point of 500 lx it was performed and described in Chapter 4: Reference building system and 
in Appendix C: Luminaries selection. As a result, the investigation process focused on the 
selection of the appropriate luminaries for every workplace, corridor and meeting room, of 
every different reference building module regarding the illuminance set point of 300 lx. The 
selection of the luminaries was based on the same calculation procedure described by the 
equations 3,4 and 5 in Chapter 4: Reference building system. In order to minimize the primary 
energy use for lighting, high efficient LED luminaries from Philips LuxSpace product series 
[25], [26] were also used. All the LED luminaries were selected with dimming function to 
provide the exact illuminance level required. The detailed selection of the luminaries for every 
workplace, corridor and meeting room for all the five different building modules and for the 
illuminance set point of 300 lx is also presented in detail in Appendix C: Luminaries selection.  
Regarding the control strategy, a simple ON/OFF operation strategy was initially used in 
the reference building modules and described analytically in Chapter 4: Reference building 
systems. Based on that strategy, the artificial lighting system was operating in the nominal 
power during the occupancy schedule and when the illuminance level due to the daylight was 
below the set point value. The illuminance level due to the daylight was identified by a light 
sensor. However, according to the results of the primary energy use for artificial lighting in 
Chapter 4: Reference building systems, the energy use of the lighting systems was higher than 
to the overall target of 25 kWh/m2 for heating, cooling, ventilation, domestic hot water and 
lighting.  
As a result, a new energy efficient control strategy was implemented for the operation of the 
lighting system in order the primary the energy use for lighting to be reduced. Through this new 
lighting control strategy, supplementary artificial lighting was provided to the available 
daylight level, through the dimming function of the luminaries, in order the illuminance set 
point to be always fulfilled during the operation schedule of the lighting system. The operation 
schedule of the lighting system was from 08:00 to 17:00 during the weekdays of the year for 
every different variation case.  
In this energy efficient control strategy, a proportional controller was created in IDA ICE in 
order to model the dimming function of the luminaries. As it was illustrated in Figure 9 of 
Chapter 4: Reference building system, there was a limitation in IDA ICE to adjust directly the 
==PAGE:56==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
56 
 
luminous flux of every luminaire because there was no direct input for the nominal luminous 
flux. It was only available to insert the nominal power and the luminous efficacy of every 
luminaire. Based on that, instead of the luminous flux, the nominal power was adjusted through 
the proportional controller, by adjusting the output digital opening signal and with the constant 
luminous efficacy, the final result was the adjustment of the luminous flux.  
In order to create the dimming function of the lighting system, the input signal of this 
proportional controller (X coordinate of the linear segments) was the illuminance level due to 
the daylight, identified by the light sensor placed in every workplace, corridor or meeting room, 
at the position of the occupant. A reverse linear function between the input illuminance level 
due to the daylight and the output digital signal (Y coordinate of the linear segments) of the 
lighting system was created through this controller. This reverse linear function was described 
by three points. In the first point, for zero illuminance level due to the daylight, the output 
digital signal of the lighting system was set to the maximum value (1 or lower value), which it 
was calculated for every zone, in the five reference building modules and for every illuminance 
set point. In the second point, for the maximum defined illuminance level due to the daylight 
(500 lx, 300 lx or 100lx) the output digital signal of the lighting system was set to zero. In the 
third point, for an unrealistic high illuminance value which it was randomly selected, the output 
electrical signal remained zero, in order to ensure that the lighting system would be closed for 
every possible illuminance level due to the daylight above the defined set point value. As a 
result, through the variable output digital signal of the lighting system according to the available 
daylight level, the nominal power of the lighting system was adjusted and by the constant 
luminous flux of every luminaire, the total luminous flux was adjusted. 
To complete the control strategy of the lighting system, the variable output signal of the 
proportional controller (between 0 and 1) was again compared with the digital output signal of 
the operative schedule (1 or 0) and the minimum value between them was the final output signal 
of the control strategy. During the non-occupancy hours, the output signal of the schedule was 
always zero. As a result, the final output digital signal of the control strategy was always zero. 
During the occupancy hours, the output signal of the schedule is always 1. As a result, the final 
digital output signal of the control strategy was always the output signal of the proportional 
controller.  
The schematic of the lighting control strategy with the proportional controller for the 
dimming function, the operation schedule and the comparator of the output signal between the 
controller and the schedule are illustrated in Figure 5.4.1. The reverse linear segment that 
defined the operation of the proportional controller for the illuminance set point of 300 lx are 
also illustrated in Figure 5.4.1. 
 
Figure 5.4.1. Illustration of control strategy of the lighting system with the proportional controller for the 
dimming function (left) and the reverse linear segment that define the operation of the proportional controller for 
300 lx as illuminance set point (right), in IDA ICE. 
In order to calculate the primary energy use for artificial lighting, energy calculations were 
performed through dynamic simulations in IDA ICE. In order to identify the complete potential

of the energy efficient control strategy related to the reduction of the primary energy use the 
shading devices were fully deactivated.  
The dynamic simulations were performed under the same climate and location conditions 
were described in Chapter 4: Reference building system, regarding every different window 
variation, for all the five different reference building modules and for the two-different 
illuminance set points.

According to the results, for the illuminance set point value of 300 lx and for the glazing 
units without solar control coating, the window variation case with the lower primary energy 
use for artificial lighting of 0.90 kWh/m2, was the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m 
length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window variation case with the higher 
primary energy use for artificial lighting of 1.54 kWh/m2, was the window arrangement 
variation of 1.2 m length for the fixed window type.  
In the variations of glazing units with solar control coating, the window variation case with 
the lower primary energy use for artificial lighting of 1.05 kWh/m2, was the window 
arrangement variation of 3 m length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window 
variation case with the higher primary energy use for artificial lighting of 1.62 kWh/m2, was 
the window arrangement variation of 1.4 m length for the fixed window type. 
For the recommended illuminance set point value of 500 lx and for the glazing units without 
solar control coating, the window variation case with the lower primary energy use for artificial 
lighting of 1.93 kWh/m2, was the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed 
window type. On the other hand, the window variation case with the higher primary energy use 
for artificial lighting of 3.20 kWh/m2, was the window arrangement variation of 1.2 m length 
for the fixed window type. 
In the variations of glazing units with solar control coating, the window variation case with 
the lower primary energy use for artificial lighting of 2.17 kWh/m2, was the window 
arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window 
variation case with the higher primary energy use for artificial lighting of 3.36 kWh/m2, was 
the window arrangement variation of 1.4 m length for the fixed window type. 
Based on the results, it was observed an average reduction of 52% in the primary energy use 
for artificial lighting regarding all the different window variation cases and for the two different 
glazing units with and without solar control coating, if the illuminance set point was reduced 
from 500 lx to 300 lx in the workplaces.

For the illuminance set point value of 500 lx and for the glazing units without solar control 
coating, the window variation case with the lower primary energy use for artificial lighting of 
2.24 kWh/m2, was the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed window 
type. On the other hand, the window variation case with the higher primary energy use for 
artificial lighting of 3.24 kWh/m2, was the window arrangement variation of 1.6 m length for 
the fixed window type. 
In the variations of glazing units with solar control coating, the window variation case with 
the lower primary energy use for artificial lighting of 2.35 kWh/m2, was the window 
arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window 
variation case with the higher primary energy use for artificial lighting of 3.18 kWh/m2, was 
the window arrangement variation of 1.8 m length for the fixed window type. 
Based on the results, it was observed an average reduction of 55% in the primary energy use 
for artificial lighting regarding all the different window variation cases and for the two different 
glazing units with and without solar control coating, if the illuminance set point was reduced 
from 500 lx to 300 lx in the workplaces. 
Based on the results for all the five different building modules, the variation case of the 
lowest primary energy use for artificial lighting was the window arrangement variation of 2.8 
m length for the fixed window type. In Table 5.5.11, an overview of the optimal primary energy 
use for lighting is presented for all the different building modules the two-different illuminance 
set point and the two glazing units, with and without solar control coating of the window 
arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed window type.

According to the results, regardless the different LT values of the glazing units and the 
different illuminance set point, the building width affected significantly the primary energy use 
for lighting, as it was expected. As long as the width of the building was increasing the primary 
energy use for artificial lighting was also increasing. In addition, for the same building width 
of 10 m, the primary energy use for lighting in the landscape building module was lower than 
the single man office module due to the higher reflectance of the mirror side walls in the 
landscape module and the presence of the partition internal walls between the office rooms in 
the single man office module.
