Chapter 6: Improved façade wall element 
6.1. Introduction 
In this Chapter, the investigation procedure is focused on improving the wall façade element, 
as it was described in Chapter 3: Methodology. Even if the reference façade wall element, as it 
was described in Chapter 4: Reference building system, it was lightweight and high insulated, 
based on the prefabrication principles, the thickness was high and as a result the daylight 
performance of the building system was limited. 
To reduce the thickness of the façade element, a new solution was introduced in the form of 
spandrel panels and replaced the reference wall element only on both facades, in every window 
variation case introduced in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting. These new spandrel panels were 
designed in order to be high insulated and lightweight but their thickness to be less than half of 
the reference façade wall element. 
A new daylight analysis was performed in all the different window variation cases of the 
five building modules in order to identify the influence of the reduced façade thickness to 
minimum Daylight Factor (DF) (%) in every permanent workplace. In addition, energy 
calculations through IDA ICE dynamic simulations were performed for all the window 
variation cases in order to identify the potential reduction of the primary energy use for lighting 
due to the reduced thickness of the façade element. 
6.2. New façade spandrel panel 
The façade panel selected to replace the reference façade wall element it was the “Futura+ 
Façade panel” constructed by IDEALCOMBI A/S [33]. The frame width of the new façade 
element was 175 mm and it was equal to the frame width of the Futura+i window elements that 
they were selected in Chapter 4: Reference building system and Chapter 5: Daylight and 
lighting. As a result, the window elements were in aligned with the spandrel panels. These 
façade panels could be used for different purposes but the main purpose according to the 
manufacturer was for concealing floor slabs and columns [33]. Several solutions were provided 
by the manufacturer for the use of this façade panel such as “sandwich panel”, “sandwich panel” 
in tilt-turn sash, fully insulated “sandwich panel” with aluminium outer side or insulating glass 
outer side and ventilated “sandwich panel” [33]. 
In order to provide a high insulated spandrel panel, similar to the reference façade wall 
element, the solution of a fully insulated “sandwich panel” was selected [33]. In this solution, 
the façade element of 30 mm thickness was enhanced by an additional insulation layer and a 
backboard of 12 mm. The total thickness of the façade panel with the additional insulation layer 
and the backboard was 175 mm as it was mentioned previously. The schematic of the fully 
insulated “Futura+ Facade panel” is illustrating in Figure 6.2.1. 
According to Figure 6.2.1, the additional insulation layer and the backboard were not 
constructed by IDEALCOMBI A/S, but they were provided by other manufacturers. As a result, 
it was assumed that the additional insulation layer was a Polyurethane (PU) foam, like the 
insulation layer of the main panel, and the backboard was constructed by medium density 
fiberboard (MDF). The thermal properties and the characteristics of the individual layers of the 
fully insulated “sandwich panel” along with the overall heat transfer coefficient (U-value) of 
the panel are presented in Table 6.2.1, according to the IDA ICE library and the Apache-Tables 
User guide of Integrated Environmental Solutions (IES) Virtual Environment [21]. 
The design of the spandrel façade panel was similar to a window element, as it is illustrated 
in Figure 6.2.1. There was a frame construction around the panel of the same width of 175 mm. 
The frame thickness on the outer side of the façade was 53 mm, while the frame thickness on 
the inner side of the façade was 50 mm. The construction of the frame was made by aluminium 
profile on the outside and wood profile on the inside. Between the aluminium and wood profile 
a high insulating PU foam was integrated [33].

As a result, there was almost no limitation concerning the implementation of the façade panel 
in the different window variation cases that they were presented in Chapter 5: Daylight and 
lighting but also in Appendix A: Window variation cases.

Figure 6.2.2. The available sizes of the “Futura+ Façade panel” [33]. 
The window elements on both facades were placed at the height of 1 m from the floor, in 
every window variation case. Based on the position of the window elements, there was a façade 
area of 3 m length and 1 m height below the window elements in every window variation case. 
As a result, two façade panels of 1.5 m length and 1 m height were placed to cover the façade 
area below the windows.  
The façade area between the window elements of two neighbor building modules, of 3 m 
façade length each, it was varied regarding the different window arrangement variation lengths. 
However, in all the cases the height of the façade panel used between the window elements was 
equal to the height of the window elements of 2 m. In the window arrangement variation of 3 
m length, there was no façade area between the window elements of two neighbor building 
modules, and no façade panel was placed. In the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m length, 
the façade length between the window elements of two neighbor building modules was 0.2 m. 
Although, the minimum available product length of the façade panel was 0.25 m, according to 
Figure 6.2.2. To overcome this limitation, it was assumed that a special construction of the 
==PAGE:67==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
67 
 
façade panel for 0.2 m length could be produced in order to cover the area between the window 
elements. Regarding the window arrangement variation of 2.6 m, 2.4 m, 2.2 m, 2 m, 1.8 m and 
1.6 m length, the length of the façade panel between the window elements of two neighbor 
building modules was 0.4 m, 0.6 m, 0.8 m, 1 m, 1.2 m and 1.4 m respectively. Regarding the 
window arrangement variation of 1.4 m and 1.2 m length, two façade panels of 0.8 m and 0.9 
m length respectively were used one next to the other, in order to cover the façade area between 
the window elements of two neighbor building modules. 
To implement the façade panels into IDA ICE models, it was assumed that the frame 
construction of the panel was not taken into account. This assumption was based on two main 
reasons. The first reason was the limitation of IDA ICE to create the external wall element as a 
panel taking into account the frame construction but also, due to the limitation of implementing 
properly a window element as a façade panel with zero LT and zero solar heat gain coefficient 
(g). The second reason was that the share of the frame area of the façade panel comparing to 
the main area of the façade panel was quite low. As a result, in order to simplify the creation of 
the different modules in IDA ICE, the façade panels were implemented as an external wall 
element based on the materials of Table 6.2.1. 
6.3. Daylight analysis and calculations regarding the primary energy use for lighting 
A supplementary daylight analysis and energy calculations through IDA ICE simulations 
were performed, in order to identify the increase of the minimum DF (%) in the workplaces 
and the reduced primary energy use for lighting, due to the reduced thickness of the façade 
panel, in all the different window variation cases. 
In order to perform the daylight analysis, the same methodology regarding the window 
arrangement variations and glazing units was used, as it was described in Chapter 5: Daylight 
and lighting. Regarding the energy calculations in order to determine the primary energy use 
for artificial lighting, the new energy efficient control strategy of the lighting system was used 
but the investigation was performed only on the illuminance set point of 500 lx. Also, the 
external blinds were deactivated in order to identify the difference in the primary energy use 
for lighting only due to the reduction of the thickness of the façade element. The rest of the 
design parameters were according to Chapter 4: Reference building system. 
6.4. Daylight analysis results 
The results regarding the minimum DF (%) in the permanent workplaces followed the same 
trend as in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting. The acceptable window variation cases regarding 
all the five building modules and the glazing units with and without solar control coating were 
marked by a green cell while the not acceptable cases were marked by a red cell. 
However, the reduce thickness of the façade panels comparing to the reference wall element, 
resulted into higher minimum DF (%) in all the workplaces. As a result, window arrangement 
variations of lower glazing area were accepted comparing to the reference wall element, in all 
the five building modules. The critical workplace was the North workplace 2 in the landscape 
building modules of 10 m and 15 m width, the North workplace 3 in the landscape building 
modules of 13 m and 18 m width and the North office in the single man offices building module 
of 10 m width. 
In Table 6.4.1 and Table 6.4.2, the daylight results regarding the reference landscape 
building module of 10 m building width are presented after the implementation of the “Futura+ 
Façade panel”.

6.5. Primary energy use for lighting 
The calculation results through IDA ICE simulations regarding the primary energy use for 
artificial lighting, after the implementation of the new “Futura+ Façade panel”, are presented 
in the following tables. The operation of the lighting system was based on the new energy 
efficient control strategy described in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting and the recommended 
illuminance set point for the workplaces and meeting room of 500 lx.  
The results regarding the primary energy use for artificial lighting were calculated for all 
the different window variation cases that were accepted based on the minimum DF 
requirements in the permanent workplaces for the glazing units with and without solar control 
coating.  
Furthermore, the primary energy use results for artificial lighting after the implementation 
of the new façade panel were compared with the primary energy use results due to the use of 
the reference façade wall element, as they were presented also in Chapter 5: Daylight and 
lighting. 
In Table 6.5.1 and Table 6.5.2, the primary energy use for artificial lighting regarding the 
window variation cases of the landscape building module of 10 m width are presented. 
According to the results after the implementation of the new façade panel for the glazing 
units without solar control coating, the window variation cases with the lower primary energy 
use for artificial lighting of 1.93 kWh/m2, were the window arrangement variation of 3 m and 
2.8 m length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window variation case with the 
higher primary energy use for artificial lighting of 3.20 kWh/m2, was the minimum examined 
variation of 1.2 m length for the fixed window type.  
In the variations of glazing units with solar control coating, the window variation case with 
the lower primary energy use for artificial lighting of 2.17 kWh/m2, was the window 
arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window 
variation case with the higher primary energy use for artificial lighting of 3.76 kWh/m2, was 
the window arrangement variation of 1.2 m length for the fixed window type. 
The results regarding the primary energy use for lighting were not influenced by the 
implementation of the new façade panel. The only reduction regarding the primary energy use 
for lighting of 1% and 2% it was noticed in the window arrangement variations of 3 m length 
for both widow types respectively. The reason for the zero reduction of the primary energy use 
for lighting was that due to the low building width and the integration of the daylight from the 
two facades, the effect of the wall thickness in this building module was insignificant. In 
addition, in all the different window variations, the light sensor position was selected to be the 
position of the first occupant. This position was by default the middle point of the zone at 0.6 
m high from the floor. As a result, this middle point was not strongly influenced by the change 
in the façade element thickness after the implementation of the new façade panel, due to the 
increased daylight level from all the neighbor window elements and the window element on 
both facades due to the low building width.

In the variations of glazing units with solar control coating, the window variation case with 
the lower primary energy use for artificial lighting of 4.78 kWh/m2, was the window 
arrangement variation of 2.8 m length for the fixed window type. On the other hand, the window 
variation case with the higher primary energy use for artificial lighting of 5.24 kWh/m2, was 
also the window arrangement variation of 2.2 m length for the fixed window type. 
Like the landscape building module of 15 m with, also in the landscape building module of 
18 m width a reduction in the primary energy use for lighting of 6% in the window arrangement 
variation case of 3 m length and a reduction of 4% in the rest of the window arrangement after 
the implementation of the new façade panel it was noticed. The reduction of the primary energy 
use for lighting was lower than the landscape building module of 15 m length because the 
operation of the lighting system in the more centralized areas was not influenced significantly 
by the limited daylight level from the façade. As a result, the change in the thickness of the 
façade element was not increased dramatically the daylight level in the more centralized areas 
and the energy use for lighting in these zones was the same as when the reference façade wall 
element was used. 
Based on the overall results for the landscape building modules of 15 m and 18 m width, it 
was observed that as the window arrangement length was decreasing, the reduction of the 
primary energy use for lighting after the implementation of the new façade panel was 
decreasing also. For the lower window arrangement sizes, the reduction of the energy use for 
lighting in the workplaces close to both facades due to the improved façade panel it was higher. 
Based on that, the energy use for lighting in the workplaces close to the facades was affected 
more by the reduction of the façade element thickness.  
However, it was observed that the reduction of the energy use for lighting in the meeting 
room followed the opposite trend comparing the workplaces. For lower window arrangement 
lengths, the reduction of the energy use for lighting in the meeting room due to the 
implementation of the new façade panel was lower. As a result, the reduction of the energy use 
for lighting was mainly affected by the reduction of the window arrangement length and not by 
the reduced thickness of the wall element. Taking into account that the energy use for lighting 
of the meeting room is the highest share of the total primary energy use for lighting of the 
building module, the trend of the meeting room was affected the trend of the whole building 
module.

Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate 
7.1. Introduction 
In this Chapter, the investigation process is focusing on the evaluation of the overall primary 
energy use for lighting, heating, cooling, ventilation and Domestic Hot Water (DHW) to be in 
compliance with the energy requirements of 2015 Danish Building Regulations of 2020 
building energy class [4]. In addition, the compliance of the indoor environment conditions 
according to the requirements of the EN 15251 [5] is investigated. 
The investigation process is performed for all the different window variation cases in the 
five different building modules, as they were described in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting, 
with the implementation of the new façade panel, as it was described in Chapter 6: Improved 
façade element. 
However, in order to minimize the primary energy use for ventilation, a new ventilation 
strategy based on Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) principles was implemented into the 
five building modules and for all the different window variation cases. Finally, in the window 
variation cases without solar control coating, the external blinds operation was again activated 
and optimized in order to reduce the solar gains and to minimize the primary energy use for 
cooling. 
7.2. Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) strategy 
As it was described in Chapter 4: Reference building system, the primary energy use for 
ventilation was a quite significant share of the overall primary energy use of the building 
modules. As a result, the optimizing of the ventilation strategy was of great importance. 
In all the reference cases, a Constant Air Volume (CAV) ventilation strategy was used. 
However, in order to improve the performance of the ventilation system, the approach of 
Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) based on a Variable Air Volume (VAV) strategy was 
implemented. The DCV system can be operate based on different signals. Some of them can be 
the CO2 concentration (ppm), the humidity level (%) or even the operative temperature (oC) 
inside a room. Taking into account that the most important indoor pollutant related to the 
occupant’s activity is the CO2 concentration (ppm) and that the ventilation system in the five 
reference building modules was used only in order to provide fresh air into the building, it was 
decided that the operation of the DCV would be related with the CO2 concentration (ppm), in 
all the five building modules. 
In order to implement the DCV approach into the different building modules, the default 
control strategy of IDA ICE was used. More specifically, the “VAV, CO2 control” option in 
IDA ICE was selected in all the different zones. Regarding the ventilation rates of the DCV 
system, the same maximum and minimum ventilation rates, as they were calculated in Table 
4.6.2 of Chapter 4: Reference building system, were used for the VAV operation of the 
ventilation system, taking into account the dilution of the indoor contaminants related to both 
occupant’s activity and building emissions. Concerning the control strategy of the VAV 
ventilation system, it was selected that the minimum ventilation flow rate of 0.7 l/sm2 related 
to the building emissions, would be provided until the CO2 concentration in every zone to reach 
850 ppm. When the CO2 concentration level was higher than 850 ppm, the maximum 
ventilation rate would be provided into the zones, so as the maximum allowed CO2 
concentration in the zones to be always lower than 900 ppm, according to EN 15251 [5]. The 
ventilation system was selected to be always in operation, by providing the minimum 
ventilation rate during the unoccupied hours of every day. 
The calculation of the primary energy use for ventilation due to the implementation of the 
DCV strategy and the evaluation of the indoor air quality were performed by dynamic 
simulations in IDA ICE with the same climate, location and occupancy conditions as they were 
described in Chapter 4: Reference building system, for all the five different building modules 
and all the different window variation cases. 
==PAGE:84==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
84 
 
7.3. Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) results 
According to the results of the dynamic simulations of IDA ICE, the primary energy use for 
ventilation and the indoor air quality were identical in all the different window variation cases 
of the five reference building modules. The reason was that the ventilation system was not used 
to provide space heating or cooling but only to supply fresh air into the zones. Also, the number 
of the occupants and the occupancy schedule in the five reference building modules was the 
same regardless the different window variation cases.  
The primary energy use for ventilation due to the use of the DCV in the five reference 
building modules is presented in Table 7.3.1 an it is compared with the primary energy use for 
ventilation when the Constant Air Volume (CAV) approach was used. 
Table 7.3.1. Primary energy use (kWh/m2) for ventilation due to the use of DCV and a comparison with the use 
of CAV, for the five reference building modules.

According to the results, there was a significant difference in all the five reference building 
modules due to the implementation of the DCV strategy. It can be noticed that as the workplace 
per occupant in the open plan areas it was increasing the difference between the use of the CAV 
and VAV ventilation strategy was decreasing.  
As a result, the implementation of the DCV is strongly recommended in the zones that the 
workplace per occupant (m2/occ) is higher because the CO2 production is lower and the 
minimum ventilation rate is provided for higher period of time, until the CO2 concentration 
(ppm) to reach the limit where the maximum ventilation rate is provided. 
In Figure 7.3.1 to Figure 7.3.3, an example of the CO2 concentration (ppm), the ventilation 
rates (l/s) provided and the age of air (h) in the zone are presented for the South workplace 1 in 
the landscape building module of 10 m width, before and after the implementation of DCV for 
a random day of the year. When the CAV ventilation strategy was used, the CO2 concentration 
(ppm) was lower than after the implementation of the DCV (VAV) strategy and the age of air 
(h) in the occupancy hours was close to the minimum limit for longer periods of time. However, 
after the implementation of the DCV, the CO2 concentration was constant, close to the 
maximum acceptable CO2 level of 900 ppm, during most of the occupancy period and as a 
result, the maximum ventilation rate (l/s) was provided into the zone for shorter period of time.  
In the rest of the workplaces of the same landscape module or in the workplaces of the other 
building modules, the CO2 concentration (ppm), the age of air (h) and the ventilation rates (l/s) 
were following the same pattern with small fluctuations, for every workday of the year. 
However, in none of the zones of the examined modules the CO2 concentration after the 
implementation of the DCV was higher than the maximum limit of 900 ppm.

Figure 7.3.2. CO2 concentration (ppm) (left) and ventilation rates (l/s) (right) in the South workplace 1 of the

Figure 7.3.3. Age of air (h) due to the use of the VAV system (left) and the use of CAV system (right) in the 
South workplace 1 of the landscape building module of 10 m width, as they were illustrated in IDA ICE. 
According to Table 7.3.1, for higher working area per occupant (m2/occ) the primary energy 
use for ventilation based on the DCV can be lowered due to the reduced CO2 concentration in 
the space. The primary energy use for ventilation could also be reduced if the number of 
occupants in the same working area was lower, due to the reduced CO2 production. 
In reality, the number of occupants in an office building is usually lower than the maximum 
designed, due to the random absent of occupants during the whole year period. In this section, 
it was assumed that every occupant of the office building was always placed in each workplace 
during all the week days of the years. In order to identify the effect of a lower occupancy in the 
office building to the performance of the ventilation system based on the DCV strategy, due to 
the absence of several office workers, a sensitivity analysis is performed in Chapter 8: 
Sensitivity analysis, by using lower occupancy level in the same open plan area of the five 
reference building modules. 
Finally, it was assumed that the ventilation system was operated with the minimum 
ventilation rate of 0.7 l/sm2 in order to minimize the CO2 concentration close to the ambient 
concentration in the unoccupied hours of the year. However, according to EN 15251 [5], in the 
unoccupied hours of the day, the ventilation flows could be reduced to 0.1 l/sm2 or 0.2 l/sm2. 
==PAGE:86==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
86 
 
Comparing to the 0.7 l/sm2 which it was used in this Chapter, the primary energy use for 
ventilation could be significantly reduced. On the other hand, the lower ventilation flows in the 
unoccupied hours could also affect the dilution of CO2 concentration, before the start of the 
occupancy schedule, because the age of air (h) would be increased due to the lower air change 
rate (ach). This change in the minimum ventilation flow rate in the DCV system is also 
evaluated in Chapter 8: Sensitivity analysis.  
7.4. Domestic Hot Water (DHW) 
The Domestic Hot Water (DHW) use, as it was described in Chapter 4: Reference building 
system, was only depended on the water use of the building regarding the floor area, and the 
temperature difference between the supplied hot water and the temperature of the cold water. 
The parameters regarding the DHW use were the same in all the five reference building 
modules, regardless the different window variation cases. As a result, in every different 
variation case examined, the primary energy use for DHW would be identical to the results of 
Chapter 4: Reference building system, of 3.14 kWh/m2. 
7.5. Heating, cooling and lighting investigation process 
In Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting and in Chapter 6: Improved façade element, different 
solutions were investigated in order to reduce the primary energy use for lighting. In this 
section, as it was described in Chapter 3: Methodology, the investigation was focused on 
reducing the primary energy use for cooling by using passive cooling solutions like external 
blinds or glazing units with solar control coating. However, the use of the external blinds or the 
glazing units with solar control coating, affected also the energy requirements for heating and 
lighting due to the reduced solar gains from the window elements and the lower available 
daylight due to the shading factor respectively. As a result, the primary energy use for lighting, 
heating and cooling was evaluated by using either external blinds or glazing units with solar 
control coating, regarding the different window variations cases of the five building modules. 
The same ideal heaters and coolers as they were described in Chapter 4: reference building 
system were used to model the mechanical heating and cooling system respectively. The 
operation of the lighting system was based on the illuminance set point of 500 lx and the new 
energy efficient lighting control strategy, which it was described in Chapter 5: Daylight and 
lighting. The new façade spandrel panes as they were described in Chapter 6: Improved façade 
elements, were also used in this part of the investigation process and finally, the DCV strategy 
was implemented in the ventilation system. The number of occupants and the occupancy 
schedule was not changed also. 
The default external blinds as they were descried in Chapter 4: Reference building system, 
were also used in this part of the investigation, regarding all the window variation cases of the 
glazing units without solar control coating. The operation strategy of the external blinds, was 
initially based on the solar irradiation level on the outer side of the window units. If the solar 
irradiation was higher than 100 W/m2, the blinds were drawn, as it was described in Chapter 4: 
Reference building system.  
However, in the beginning of the investigation procedure regarding the evaluation of the 
overall primary energy use of the different window variation cases with the external blinds, an 
short investigation was performed in order to identify the optimal irradiation set point (W/m2) 
for the operation of the external blinds, in the five different building modules. In the building 
modules with higher width, the use of a low irradiation set point could reduce significantly the 
available daylight in the more centralized areas and as a result, the primary energy use for 
lighting would be higher. Different solar irradiation set point values were implemented in the 
five reference building modules for the window arrangement variation of 2.6 m length. The 
examined solar irradiation set point values in all the five building modules were 100 W/m2, 200 
W/m2 and 300 w/m2. In addition, in the landscape building module of 10 m width and in the 
single man office building module of 10 m width, the set point value of 50 W/m2 was also 
examined, due to the lower building width. Also, in the landscape building module of 18 m 
==PAGE:87==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
87 
 
width, the setpoint value of 400 W/m2 was examined due to the higher building width. The 
setpoint value which resulted into the lower value for the total primary energy use for lighting, 
heating and cooling was selected in every different building module, for all the different 
window variation cases examined. 
Also, the total primary energy use results for lighting, heating and cooling were investigated 
for the window variation cases that the glazing units with solar control coating were used.  
Finally, the results regarding several important parameters of the indoor environment and 
examples regarding the operation of the different systems are presented in this Chapter. The 
calculation of the primary energy use and the evaluation of the indoor climate conditions for 
the different variation cases, it was performed once again through IDA ICE dynamic 
simulations for the same location and climatic conditions as it was described in Chapter 4: 
Reference building system.

According to the results, the optimal solar irradiation set point of the landscape building 
module of 10 m, 13 m, 15 m and 18 m width was 100 W/m2, 200 W/m2, 200 W/m2 and 300 
W/m2 respectively. The optimal solar irradiation set point in the single man office building 
module was 200 W/m2. These specific set point values were used for the operation of the 
external blinds in all the different window variation cases of the same building modules 
respectively. Based on Table 7.6.1 to Table 7.6.5, as the building width was increasing the 
optimal solar irradiation set point was also increasing, due to the reduction of the available 
daylight in the more centralized areas in wider building modules. 
In Table 7.6.6 to Table 7.6.9, the primary energy use results for lighting, heating and cooling, 
the total primary energy use of these three systems and the overall primary energy use taking 
into account also the primary energy use for ventilation and DHW, due to the use of the external 
blinds, are presented for the variations of the four landscape buildings of differed width. 
Based on the results regarding all the different window variations, it was noticed that as the 
glazing area of the window arrangements was decreasing, the primary energy use for heating 
and cooling was decreasing, while the primary energy use for lighting was increasing. However, 
the sum of the primary energy use of these three systems was decreasing along with the glazing 
area up to a minimum value. This minimum value was not always related with the minimum 
glazing area examined.

In the landscape building module of 10 m width, the minimum value of the total primary 
energy use for lighting heating and cooling of 8.73 kWh/m2 it was noticed in the window 
arrangement variation of 1.6 m length, for fixed window type. As a result, the window 
arrangement variation cases of fixed window type and lower length of 1.4 m and 1.2 m were 
resulted into higher primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling, because the increase 
of the primary energy use for lighting was exceeding the reduction of the primary energy use 
for heating and cooling. The same phenomenon it was noticed also in the landscape building 
modules of 15 m width and in the single man offices building module of 10 m width. The 
minimum primary energy use for lighting heating and cooling in these building modules was 
9.62 kWh/m2 and 9.81 kWh/m2 respectively and they were noticed in the same window 
arrangement variation case of 1.6 m length, for fixed window type. In the rest of the building 
modules, the minimum primary energy use was related to the minimum window arrangement 
variation of fixed window type examined, where the window arrangement length was equal or 
higher than 1.6 m.

Comparing the use of the window elements of the tilt-turn type in contrast with the fixed 
type in the same window arrangement variation, it was noticed that the total primary energy 
use of lighting heating and cooling it was lower when the fixed window elements were used. 
More specifically, the primary energy use for lighting and heating was higher when the tilt-turn 
window type was used, while the primary energy use for cooling was higher when the fixed 
window type was used. The reason was the difference in the frame area (m2) which it was higher 
in the tilt-turn window variations. As a result, due to the higher U-value (W/m2K) of the frame 
element comparing to the glazing unit, the higher frame area resulted into higher heat 
transmition through the frame and as a result, the primary energy use for heating was increased 
while the primary energy use for cooling was reduced. On the other hand, the increased frame 
area for the same window arrangement, resulted into lower glazing area and as a result, the 
primary energy use for lighting was higher.

If we compare the landscape building modules of 10 m and 15 m width, of two workplaces 
close to the facades, with the landscape modules of 13 m and 18 m width, of three workplaces 
close to the facades, it was noticed that the primary energy use for lighting heating and cooling 
was quite lower in the cases of three workplaces. In the building modules of tree workplaces, 
the primary energy use for lighting was higher due to the increased building width. Also, the 
primary energy use for cooling was higher and the primary energy use for heating was lower 
due to the higher internal gains related with the number of occupants. However, the decrees of 
the primary energy use for heating due to the increased internal gains was quite significant and 
higher than the decrease of the primary energy use for both lighting and cooling. 
As a result, it can be concluded that for the examined landscape building modules, when the 
external blinds were used, the differences in the primary energy use for heating and cooling due 
to the internal gains related to the different number of occupants were more significant than the 
differences in the primary energy use for artificial lighting due to the increase of the building 
width.  
On the other hand, when landscape building modules of the same number of workplaces but 
different building widths, due to the presence of the meeting room, were compared, it was 
noticed that for the same window arrangement variations, the primary energy use for lighting 
heating and cooling it was higher as the building width was increased mainly due to the increase 
of the primary energy use for lighting. More specifically, for the wider building modules, the 
increase in the primary energy use for lighting was quite significant, while the primary energy 
use for heating and cooling was reduced due to the same internal gains in the higher building 
floor area. Furthermore, this difference in the total primary energy use was higher as the glazing 
area of the window variation cases was decreasing.  
As a result, for the same number of occupants in a building module, it was concluded that 
the increase of the primary energy use for lighting due to the increase of the building width was 
==PAGE:91==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
91 
 
quite significant than the reduction of the primary energy use for heating and cooling due to the 
higher building floor area. 
In all the landscape building modules, the implementation of the 4-pane glazing unit in the 
window arrangement variation of 3 m length resulted into lower primary energy use for lighting 
heating and cooling comparing to the window arrangement variation of 2.8 m length where the 
glazing area was lower and the 3-pane glazing unit was used. More specifically, the primary 
energy use for lighting between the two window variations was almost the same in the different 
building modules. Based on this fact, the increase of the glazing area in the window 
arrangement variation of 3 m length eliminated the effect of the 4% LT reduction of the glazing 
unit due to addition of the fourth glass pane. In addition, the primary energy use for cooling 
was higher in the cases where the 4-pane glazing unit was used. The 2% reduction of the solar 
heat gain coefficient (g) in the 4-pane glazing unit, was insignificant comparing to the increase 
of the glazing area from the window arrangement of 2.8 m length to 3 m length. The most 
important factor that explained the reduction in the total primary energy use, was the significant 
reduction of the primary energy use for heating. The U-value of the 4-pane glazing element was 
0.36 W/m2K comparing to the U-value of the default glazing unit of 0.52 W/m2K. 
As a result, if the 4-pane glazing was used in the rest of the window arrangement variations 
by increasing the arrangement length at 0.2 m, the primary energy use for lighting would be the 
same but the energy use for heating would be significantly lower. In addition, the increased 
glazing area could improve the occupant’s satisfaction with their working environment by 
provide them sufficient outside view. 
Regarding the overall primary energy use for lighting, heating, cooling, ventilation and 
DHW, in the four landscape building modules, it was noticed that most of the examined window 
variation cases were fulfilling maximum primary energy use requirements of 25 kWh/m2 [4]. 
In the landscape building module of 10 m, 13 m and 15 m width, the window variation cases 
of 2.8 m length resulted into higher than 25 kWh/m2 overall primary energy use result. Based 
on these results, the use of the 4-pane glazing unit would be suggested also in the window 
arrangement variations of 2.8 m width.

primary energy use of these three systems and the overall primary energy use taking into 
account also the primary energy use for ventilation and DHW, due to the use of the external 
blinds, are presented for the single man office building module variation.

Comparing the results between the single man office building module with the landscape 
building module of 10 m width, it was noticed that the primary energy use for lighting was 
slightly lower in the single man office, due to the higher solar irradiation set point was selected 
for the operation of the external blinds. However, this selection of the higher solar irradiation 
set point, affected also the energy balance between the primary energy use for heating and 
cooling. The energy requirements for heating due to the lower number of occupants should be 
quite higher in the single man office module. Although, the increased solar gains due to the 
higher solar irradiation set point, minimized the primary energy use for heating in the single 
man office building module. Even so, the primary energy use for heating it was higher in the 
single man office modules than the landscape module. On the other hand, a disadvantage of the 
higher irradiation set point in order to minimize the primary energy use for lighting and heating 
in the single man office module, was the significant increase of the primary energy use for 
cooling. However, the total primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling in the 
landscape building module of 10 m width was significantly lower.  
In Figure 7.6.1 and Figure 7.6.2, the available daylight level during the year period is 
illustrated in the two South workplaces of the slim landscape building module of 10 m width in 
contrast with the three South workplaces of the wider landscape module of 18 m width. In both 
different building modules, the same window arrangement of 2.6 m width was used.

blinds, in the South workplace 1 (right) and the more centralized South workplace 2 (right), of the landscape 
building module of 10 m width and the window arrangement of 2.6 m length during the year.

An example of the operation of the shading device and the effect in the available daylight 
and the operation of the lighting system in the South Workplace 1 and 2 of the landscape 
building module of 10 m width are illustrated in Figure 7.6.3 to Figure 7.6.5, for a critical day 
of the year, on 5th of July. 
 
Figure 7.6.3. Operation signal of the external blinds of the window elements on the South façade. For 
operation signal of 1, the external blinds are completely drawn, while for operation signal of 0, the blinds are not 
drawn. 
 
Figure 7.6.4. Illuminance level (lux) due to the daylight only, at the position of the first occupant, in the South 
workplace 1 (left) and the South workplace 2 (right), based on the operation of the external blinds of the window 
elements of the South façade. 
 
Figure 7.6.5. The total power (W) which is used by the lighting system of the South workplace 1 (left) and 
South workplace 2 (right), based on the operation of the external blinds and the dimming function of the lighting 
system. 
Based on Figure 7.6.3, the external blinds were drawn from 8:40 to 11:00 and form 15:00 
to 17:20 on the 5th of July. According to the operation of the external blinds, the illuminance 
level (lx) due to the daylight through the windows in the South workplace 1 closer to the façade, 
was increasing from 0 lx at 04:30 in the morning, to almost 2400 lx, just before the first drawn 
of the external blinds at 08:40. In the more centralized South workplace 2, the illuminance level 
(lx) due to the daylight was increasing also from 0 lx at the 04:30 to 800 lx just before the first 
drawn of the external blinds at 08:40. When the blinds were drawn, the illuminance level in the 
South workplace 1 and 2 was reduced instantly from 2400 lx and 800 lx respectively to around 
400 lx and 250 lx respectively in the two zones. 
Regarding the operation of the lighting system in Figure 7.6.5, from the beginning of the 
operation schedule at 8:00 to 8:40, the lighting system was not in operation in both zones, 
because the illuminance level due to the daylight was higher than the illuminance set point of 
==PAGE:94==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
94 
 
500 lx. When the external blinds were drawn at 8:40 and the illuminance level due to the 
daylight was dropped to 400 lx and 250 lx in the two zones, the lighting system through the 
dimming function started to operate. The maximum designed nominal power of the lighting 
system in both zones was 28 W and the luminous efficacy of the lighting system was 107 lm/W. 
the floor area of both zones was 6 m2. As a result, at 8:40 exactly, the illuminance level due to 
the daylight was 352 lx in South workplace 1 and 238 lx in South workplace 2. At the same 
time, the nominal power of the lighting system was 8.3 W and 14.7 W in the two zones 
respectively. Based on the same illuminous flux of 107 lm/W, the lumens provided inside the 
two zones from the lighting system were 888 lm and 1573 lm respectively. Taking into account 
the floor area of the two workplaces of 6 m2, the illuminance due to the lighting system was 
148 lx and 262 lx. As a result, at 8:40 in the morning, the illuminance level due to the available 
daylight and due to the operation of the lighting system was 500 lx in both zones. 
It should be mentioned that the extremely high illuminance level during the occupied hours 
when the external blinds were not drawn, could cause glare discomfort to the occupants that 
could be eliminated by the additional use of internal curtains.

and the operation of the lighting system in the South Workplace 1, 2 and 3 of the landscape 
building module of 18 m width are illustrated in Figure 7.6.6 to Figure 7.6.8, for a critical day 
of the year on 7th of July.

drawn. 
 
 
Figure 7.6.7. Illuminance level (lux) due to the daylight only, at the position of the first occupant, in the South 
workplace 1 (left), the South workplace 2 (right) and the South workplace 3 (bottom), based on the operation of

Figure 7.6.8. The total power (W) which is used by the lighting system of the South workplace 1 (left), South 
workplace 2 (right) and South Workplace 3 (bottom), based on the operation of the external blinds and the 
dimming function of the lighting system. 
As it was described for the landscape building module of 10 m width, the operation of the 
external blinds reduced the daylight level from around 8000 lx to 1000 lx in the South 
workplace 1, from 2000 lx to 250 lx in the South workplace 2 and from 800 lx to 200 lx in the 
South workplace 3, regarding the landscape building module of 18 m width. The operation of 
the lighting system it was similar to the landscape building module of 10 m width. However, 
the maximum designed nominal power of the lighting system in the three zones was 22 W and 
the luminous efficacy of the lighting system was 123 lm/W. The floor area of the zones was 5.4 
m2.  Due to the fact that the illuminance set point of the lighting system was always 500 lx in 
the workplaces, the lighting system in the South workplace 1 was completely deactivated 
because the illuminance due to the daylight was always higher than 500 lx. The lighting system 
in the South workplace 2 was proportionally activated when the external blinds were drawn and 
the illuminance level due to the daylight was lower than 500 lx. In the South Workplace 3, the 
lighting system was proportionally activated from the beginning of the occupancy schedule 
until the illuminance level due to the daylight reached 500 lx at 9:00 and it was in operation 
again when the external blinds were drawn and the illuminance level due to the daylight was 
dropped below 500 lx.  
In Figure 7.6.9 to Figure 7.6.11, the operative temperature of South workplace 1 and South 
workplace 2 of the landscape building module of 10 m width and for the window arrangement 
variation of 2.6 m length are illustrated in three different weeks of the year. The first week was 
from 23rd of January to 29th of January, the second week was from 10th of April to 16th of April 
and the last week was from 3rd of July to 9th of July. Based on the results, the range of the 
operative temperature maximum and minimum values in the more centralizes South workplace 
2, is slightly shorter due to the higher distance for the façade, which decrease slightly the effect 
of the direct solar gains and the heat losses through the façade.

According to the results, in the wider landscape building module of 18 m width, the 
operative temperature range was higher comparing to the landscape building module of 10 m 
width, due to the higher solar gains based on the higher solar irradiation set point value of the 
external blinds. In addition, as the distance from the façade was increasing, for most of the year 
period, the operative temperature range was lower in the more centralized zones due to the 
lower effect of the direct solar gains from the window elements. In the winter months of the 
year, the minimum operative temperature values in the more centralized zones were higher 
comparing to the zones close to the facades because the effect of the heat losses through the 
façade was lower. Finally, in the summer months, the operative temperature of the zones close 
to the façade was reaching the cooling set point value for higher period of time due to the effect 
of the direct solar gains in the zone. 
7.7. Primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling due to the use of glazing units 
with solar control coating 
In Table 7.7.1 to Table 7.7.4, the primary energy use results for lighting, heating and cooling, 
the total primary energy use of these three systems and the overall primary energy use taking 
into account also the primary energy use for ventilation and DHW, due to the use of the glazing 
units with solar control coating, are presented for the variations of the four landscape buildings 
of differed width. 
As the building width was increasing the primary energy use for cooling was decreasing due 
to the lower effect of the direct solar gains to the more centralized areas of the buildings. Taking 
into account the increase of the primary energy use for lighting and the reduce of the primary 
energy use for heating as the building width was increasing, the selected glazing units were 
more suitable in the building modules of an average width between the extreme examined cases.

In addition, as the building width was decreasing and the glazing area was increasing, it was 
noticed that there was an unbalance between the primary energy use for lighting, comparing 
with the primary energy use for cooling which it was extremely high. This fact indicated that 
the selected glazing units of 66% LT and solar heat gain coefficient (g) of 36% for the 3-pane 
glazing unit and 61% LT and solar heat gain coefficient (g) of 34% were probably not suitable 
for the window variation cases of higher glazing area. As a result, in Chapter 8: Sensitivity 
analysis, new glazing units with lower LT and solar heat gain coefficient (g) are implemented 
into the variations with glazing units with solar control coating in order a balance between the 
primary energy use for lighting and cooling to be achieved in the variation cases of higher 
glazing area and the overall results in the primary energy use for the rest of the systems to be 
evaluated. 
Furthermore, it was noticed that as the glazing area was decreasing the increase of the 
primary energy use for cooling was insignificant comparing to the decrease of the primary 
energy use for cooling. As a result, for the specific glazing units implemented into the different 
window variations, the lower glazing areas were suitable.

the cases the overall primary energy use was exceeding the maximum energy requirement of

were for the window arrangement length of 1.6 m, 1.4 m and 1.2 m. In the landscape building

based on the daylight analysis results, it was below the maximum energy requirement of 25 
kWh/m2. The highest number of acceptable window variations according to the energy 
requirements was in the landscape building modules of 15 m width as it was illustrated in Table 
14. In the landscape building variation of 18 m width, there was no acceptable window variation 
case according to the maximum energy requirements of 25 kWh/m2.

In the single man office building module, the primary energy use for lighting was higher 
than the landscape building modules of the same width as it was explained in Chapter 5: 
Daylight and lighting and Chapter 6: Improved façade element. The primary energy use for 
cooling was also higher due to the reduced internal gains related to the number of occupants in 
the office rooms. However, the primary energy use for cooling was also higher in the single 
man office building module due to the lower air change rate related to the lower ventilation 
rates during the occupancy schedule. The age of air (h) in the office room was higher comparing 
to the landscape building module and as a result, the air was heated due to the internal gains 
through the windows. In addition, the presence of the partition internal walls between the 
corridor in the single man office module increased the heat storage in the internal walls and as 
a result more cooling power it was required in order the operative temperature to be below the 
cooling set point.

in contrast with the three South workplaces of the wider landscape module of 18 m width when 
the glazing units with solar control coating were used. In both different building modules, the 
same window arrangement of 2.6 m width was used.

Based on Figure 7.7.3, the illuminance level (lx) due to the daylight through the windows 
in the South workplace 1 closer to the façade and the South workplace 2, it was increasing from 
75 lx and from 50 lx at the beginning of the occupancy schedule, to reach the maximum value 
of 393 lx and 134 lx at 12:30 respectively in the two zones. On the other hand, the lighting 
system was started to operate at 23.9 W in the South workplace 1 and 24.5 in the South 
workplace 2, in order to decrease proportionally to 6 W and 20.5 W respectively at 12:30. The 
maximum designed nominal power of the lighting system in both zones was 28 W and the 
luminous efficacy of the lighting system was 107 lm/W. The floor area of both zones was 6 m2. 
As a result, at 12:30 the illuminance level due to the lighting system was 107 lx and 366 lx in 
the two zones respectively in order the total illuminance level based on both daylight and 
lighting to be exactly 500 lx.

lighting system in both zones was 22 W and the luminous efficacy of the lighting system was 
123 lm/W. The floor area of both zones was 5.4 m2. As a result, the illuminance level due to 
the lighting system at 12:30 was 96 lx, 371 lx and 417 lx in the three zones respectively in order 
the total illuminance level due to both daylight and lighting to be 500 lx.

According to the results, in the wider landscape building module of 18 m width, the 
operative temperature range was higher comparing to the landscape building module of 10 m 
width, due to the higher internal gains related to the number of occupants. In addition, as the 
distance from the façade was increasing, for most of the year period, the operative temperature 
range was lower in the more centralized zones due to the lower effect of the direct solar gains 
from the window elements. In the winter months of the year, the minimum operative 
temperature values in the more centralized zones were higher comparing to the zones close to 
the facades because the effect of the heat losses through the façade was lower. Finally, in the 
summer months, the operative temperature of the zones close to the façade was reaching the 
cooling set point value for higher period of time due to the effect of the direct solar gains in the 
zone.

Chapter 8: Sensitivity analysis 
8.1. Introduction 
In this Chapter, four different sensitivity analyses are performed regarding different 
parameters that were remained constant in the investigation process so far. The first parameter 
of the sensitivity analysis was the number of occupants in the four different building modules 
examined. The second parameter was the minimum ventilation rate used regarding the 
operation of the ventilation system during the unoccupied hours of the year. The third parameter 
was the Light Transmittance (LT) and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) of the glazing units 
with solar control coating in relation with the energy requirements for cooling. The parameter 
that was the subject of the final sensitivity analysis was the position of the examined building 
modules in the multi-storey office building module. 
8.2. Sensitivity analysis based on the number of occupants in the building modules 
In Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate, the importance of the internal gains in 
the building modules was identified. In the building modules of lower number of workplaces 
and occupants, the primary energy for heating was significantly higher than the primary energy 
use for cooling, while in the building modules of higher number of occupants, the primary 
energy use for heating and cooling was more balanced and lower comparing to the previous 
case. 
In addition, based on the Demand controlled Ventilation (DCV) approach of the ventilation 
system, the primary energy use for ventilation was higher when the number of the occupants 
was also higher, due to the reduction of the available workplace per occupant (m2/occ) in the 
open plan areas of the building modules. Based on these two conclusions, the different number 
of occupants in the same building modules could differentiate the results regarding the primary 
energy use for heating, cooling and ventilation.  
As it was described in Chapter 3: Methodology, office buildings are designed in order to 
operate with a maximum capacity regarding the number of occupants in the building. However, 
in real operation conditions the number of occupants in an office building system is always 
lower than the design maximum capacity due to the random absent of the occupants during 
different days or periods of the year. As a result, it is quite common that office building systems 
are operating in a daily basis with lower than the designed number of occupants. This reduction 
in the number of occupants of an office building system could affect the primary energy use 
requirements of the building system. 
In the sensitivity analysis regarding the number of occupants in the building modules, two 
different variations cases were investigated. One case of 75% occupancy level and another case 
of 50% occupancy level in each of the five different building modules examined, comparing to 
the case of the maximum designed occupancy level of 100%, which it was examined in Chapter 
7: Primary energy use and indoor climate. The occupants in the different building modules in 
IDA ICE were placed in permanent workplaces. However, the maximum ventilation rates were 
calculated taking into account that the workplaces and the corridors were part of an open plan 
area with the same conditions regarding the ventilation rates (l/s). As a result, it was decided in 
IDA ICE to equally reduce the number of occupants in the different workplaces so as the 
distribution of the occupants in the different zones not to be changed and the results to be 
comparable. In the case of the maximum designed occupancy level, one occupant was placed 
in each workplace. In the variation case of 75% occupancy level, the unrealistic number of 0.75 
occupants was used in each workplace, while in the case of 50% occupancy level, 0.5 occupants 
were placed in each workplace, in order the distribution regarding the different workplaces to 
be unchanged. In the same way, the number of equipment related to the number of occupants 
was equally reduced. 
The maximum and minimum ventilation rates (l/s) as they were calculated in Chapter 4: 
Reference building system, were remained unchanged. The DCV approach that it was described 
in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate, was also used for the sensitivity analysis. 
==PAGE:107==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
107 
 
The rest of the designed parameters were used for the evaluation of the five building modules 
in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate, were also remained unchanged. The 
calculation of the primary energy use in the different variation of the five building modules was 
once again performed through dynamic simulations in IDA ICE based on the same conditions 
as they were described in Chapter 4: Reference building system. 
8.3. Primary energy use results for 75% number of occupants in the different building 
modules 
The primary energy use for ventilation in the five different building modules was the same 
regarding the different window variation cases examined, because the ventilation system was 
used only to provide fresh air and not for space heating or cooling. It was only depended on the 
number of occupants, on the occupancy schedule and the production of CO2 in the different 
zones. 
The primary energy use for ventilation in the five different building modules for 75% 
occupancy level and the use of DCV based on CO2 concentration (ppm) are presented in Table 
8.3.1, and they are compared with the results of 100% occupancy level. 
Table 8.3.1. Primary energy use (kWh/m2) for ventilation due to the use of 75% occupancy level and the use of 
the DCV. A comparison with the design 100% occupancy level, for the five reference building modules is also 
performed.

According to Table 8.3.1, for the 100% occupancy level, as the workplace per occupant was 
decreasing the primary energy use for ventilation was increasing. In addition, the building 
modules of 15 and 18 m width, with meeting room in the center, resulted in less primary energy 
use than the modules of 10 m and 13 m without meeting room, due to the minimum energy 
requirement of the unoccupied meeting room divided with the total floor area. 
However, for 75% occupancy level, between the building modules of 10 m width and 15 m 
width that was not the case. Even if the workplace per occupant was higher in the building 
module of 15 m width, the primary energy use was slightly higher. This result can be explained 
according to the fact that the reduction in the primary energy use for ventilation in the open 
plan areas of the workplaces and corridors, due to the reduction of the number of occupants, it 
was higher than the minimum additional energy requirements for ventilation of the unoccupied 
meeting room, between the two different building modules, divided with the higher floor area. 
This resulted into higher primary energy use for ventilation in the landscape building module 
with the meeting room. On the other hand, between the landscape building modules of 13 m 
width and 18 m width, the reduction of the primary energy use for ventilation due to the reduced 
number of occupants was lower than the additional energy use requirements of the unoccupied 
meeting room, divided also with the higher floor area. As a result, the primary energy use for 
ventilation in the building module of 18 m width was lower than the module of 13 m width. 
In Figure 8.3.1 and Figure 8.3.2, the CO2 concentration (ppm) and the ventilation rates (l/s) 
in the South workplace 1 of the landscape building module of 10 m width, between the cases 
of 100% occupancy and 75% occupancy level, for a typical day of the year.

Figure 8.3.2. CO2 concentration (ppm) (left) and ventilation rates (l/s) (right) in the South workplace 1 of the 
landscape building module of 10 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 75% occupancy level, as they

Taking into account that the input of the maximum ventilation rate was calculated in Chapter 
4: Reference building system to be 1.63 l/sm2 and that the floor area of the zone was 6m2, the 
maximum ventilation rate should be 9.78 l/s. Although, due to the lower CO2 production related 
to the lower number of occupants, the maximum ventilation flow used from the ventilation 
system was 7.75 l/s. This ventilation flow was provided in the zone when the CO2 concentration 
was above 850 ppm at 9:00 in the morning. As a result, for the first hour of the occupancy 
schedule the minimum ventilation rate of 0.7 l/sm2 (4.2 l/s) related to the dilution of the building 
emissions was provided in the zone. Regarding the CO2 concentration, it was stable at 880 ppm 
regarding most of the occupancy schedule. It was not reached the concentration of 900 ppm 
due to the nature of the default VAV based on CO2 strategy it was used in IDA ICE.  
In the rest of the different examined building modules, the CO2 concentration and the 
ventilation rates were following the same trend as in the landscape building module of 10 m 
width. In the meeting rooms, due to zero occupants the ventilation rate was always related to 
the building emissions. 
In Table 8.3.2 to Table 8.3.6, the primary energy use results for heating and cooling, is 
presented for the different window variations of the five different building modules, due to the 
use of the external blinds and for 75% occupancy level. The results are compared with the 
primary energy use for heating and cooling for 100% occupancy level. 
According to the results, the primary energy use for heating and cooling was higher when 
the number of occupants was reduced from 100% to 75%, in almost every of the window 
variations examined in the five different building modules. This increase was more significant 
in the landscape building modules of two workplaces close to the facades. The internal gains 
related to the number of occupants were lower in the building modules of two workplaces close 
to the facades. As a result, the increase in the primary energy use for heating when the 
occupancy level was reduced from 100% to 75% it was higher than the reduction of the primary 
energy use for cooling, comparing to the landscape building modules of three workplaces close 
to the facades.

solar gains from the window elements, the difference in the internal gains was more significant 
regarding the energy requirements for heating and cooling in the overall energy balance.

In the building modules with the same number of workplaces, the increase in the primary 
energy use for heating and cooling was slightly higher as the building width was increasing. 
The reason was that the heat transfer through the façade elements was the same but the increase 
of the floor area due to the addition of the meeting room, reduced the effect of the direct solar 
gains into the more centralized areas of the building modules. As a result, due to the lower 
effect of the solar gains in the center of the wider building modules, the heating requirements 
were slightly higher in the colder days of the year.

When the 4-pane glazing unit was used, in the window arrangement variations of 3 m length, 
the increase in the primary energy use for heating and cooling was lower than the window 
arrangement variation of 2.8 m length where the 3-pane glazing unit was used. The lower U-
value of the 4-pane units reduced the heat losses through the widow element. In addition, the 
higher glazing area when the 4-pane glazing units were used increase the solar gains. As a 
result, when the occupancy level was reduced from 100% to 75%, the increase in the primary 
energy use for heating it was higher in the case of the 3-pane glazing unit, while the reduction 
in the primary energy use for cooling was almost the same between the two glazing units. 
In most of the examined window variations, the use of the tilt-turn window element was 
resulted into higher primary energy use for heating and cooling. The higher frame area on the 
façade when the tilt-turn window elements were used, resulted into higher heat losses, because 
the U-value of the frame was significantly higher than the U-value of the glazing area. Due to 
the higher heat losses related to the tilt-turn windows use, the reduction of the internal gains 
related to the number of occupants highly affected the energy balance and as a result, the energy 
requirements for heating were further increased comparing to the use of the fixed window 
elements.

Concerning the single man office building module of 10 m width, the increase in the primary 
energy use for heating and cooling after the reduction of the number of occupants was lower 
comparing to the landscape building module of the same width. The reason was the higher solar 
irradiation set point value for the operation of the external blinds in the single man office 
building module, comparing to the landscape building module of 10 m width. Due to the higher 
solar irradiation set point, the solar gains were higher and the energy requirements for heating 
were limited. As a result, the reduction of the internal gains related to the number of occupants 
was of less importance in the energy balance of the single man office building module, 
comparing to the landscape building module of the same width. 
In Table 8.3.7 to Table 8.3.11, the primary energy use for heating and cooling, is presented 
for the different window variations of the five different building modules, due to the use of the 
glazing units with solar control coating, for 75% occupancy. The results are also compared with 
the primary energy use for these two systems when the occupancy was 100%. 
By looking at the results, the solar gains due to the use of the glazing units with solar control 
coating were significantly higher than the solar gains due to the use of the dynamic external 
blinds. Based on this fact, in all the different cases examined, the solar gains were more 
significant in the energy balance of the different building modules than the internal gains. 
The increase in the primary energy use for heating due to the reduction of the number of 
occupants, was lower than the reduction of the primary energy use for cooling, in most of the 
different window variations of the landscape building modules of 10 m, 13 m and 18 m width. 
On the other hand, in the most of the widow variations examined in the landscape building 
module of 15 m width and the single man office building module of 10 m, the increase in the 
primary energy use for heating was higher than the reduction of the primary energy use for 
cooling due to the reduction of the number of occupants. 
In the building modules that the reduction of the primary energy use for cooling was higher 
than the increase of the primary energy use for heating, after the reduction of the number of 
occupants, as the glazing area was decreasing, the reduction of the primary energy use for 
heating was more significant than the reduction in the primary energy use for cooling due to 
the lower glazing area. On the other hand, in the building modules that that the reduction of the 
primary energy use for cooling was lower than the increase of the primary energy use for 
heating, as the glazing area was decreasing the reduction of the primary energy use for cooling 
==PAGE:112==

was more significant than the reduction in the primary energy use for heating due to the lower 
glazing area.  
In the building modules of two workplaces close to the façade, the decrease in the primary 
energy use for cooling, due to the reduction of the internal gains, was lower comparing to the 
building modules of three workplaces. In addition, as the building width was increasing, the 
increase of the primary energy use for heating, due to the reduction of the internal gains, was 
higher than the reduction of the primary energy use for cooling, because the direct solar gains 
less affected the more centralized areas of the building.

In the single man office building module, the increase of the primary energy use for heating, 
due to the reduced internal gains, was higher than the reduction of the primary energy use for 
cooling comparing with the landscape building module of the same width. The reason was the 
lower number of occupants between the two different building modules.   
8.4. Primary energy use results for 50% number of occupants in the different building 
modules 
Like the variation case of 75% occupancy level, the primary energy use for ventilation in 
the five different building modules was the same regarding the different window variation cases 
examined. It was only depended on the CO2 production in the different zones related to the 
number of occupants and the occupancy schedule. 
The primary energy use for ventilation in the five different building modules for 50% 
occupancy level and the use of DCV based on CO2 concentration (ppm) are presented in Table 
8.4.1, and they are compared with the results of 100% occupancy level.

According to Table 12, in contrast with the results of the 75% occupancy level, for 50% 
occupancy level, the primary energy use of the two building modules with meeting rooms in 
the center (15 m and 18 m) was higher than the primary energy use of the building modules 
without meeting room (10 m and 13 m). The reason once again was that the reduction in the 
primary energy use for ventilation in the open plan areas of workplaces and corridors due to the 
reduction of the number of occupants, was higher than the minimum additional energy 
requirements for ventilation of the unoccupied meeting room between the two different building 
modules, divided with the higher floor area. This resulted into higher primary energy use for 
ventilation in the landscape building module with the meeting room. 
In Figure 8.4.1 and Figure 8.4.2, the CO2 concentration (ppm) and the ventilation rates (l/s) 
in the South workplace 1 of the landscape building module of 10 m width, between the cases 
of 100% occupancy and 50% occupancy, for a typical day of the year.

landscape building module of 10 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 100% occupancy level, as 
they were illustrated in IDA ICE.

Figure 8.4.2. CO2 concentration (ppm) (left) and ventilation rates (l/s) (right) in the South workplace 1 of the 
landscape building module of 10 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 50% occupancy level, as they 
were illustrated in IDA ICE. 
When the occupancy level was reduced from the designed 100% to 50%, the maximum 
ventilation flow rate (l/s) used by the VAV operation of the ventilation system was reduced 
from 9.78 l/s to 5.2 l/s, taking into account that the maximum designed ventilation rate was 1.63 
l/sm2 and that the floor area of the zone was 6m2. In addition, the minimum ventilation flow of 
0.7 l/sm2 (4.2 l/s), was provided for higher period of time in the beginning of the occupancy 
schedule for the reduced number of occupants, until 10:20. The maximum CO2 concentration 
for 50% occupancy in the zone was slightly lower than the previous case of 100% and 75%, 
close to 850 ppm, in contrast with 900 ppm and 880 ppm in the two previous cases respectively. 
In the rest of the different examined building modules the CO2 concentration and the 
ventilation rates were following the same trend as in the landscape building module of 10 m 
width. In the meeting rooms, due to zero occupants the ventilation rate was always related to 
the building emissions.

of the five different building modules due to the use of the external blinds, for 50% occupancy 
level and a comparison with the results for 100% occupancy level is performed.

According to the results, like the variation case of 75% occupancy level, the total primary 
energy use for heating and cooling in all the window variations examined in the five different 
building modules with the use of the external blinds was higher when the number of occupants 
was decreasing from 100% to 50%. The limited solar gains due to the effective operation of the 
external blinds combined with the higher reduction of the number of occupants, resulted into 
higher primary energy use for heating comparing to the reduction of the primary energy use for 
cooling, than the case of 75% occupancy. In general, the results were following the same trend 
as the variation case of 75% occupancy but the difference in the primary energy use for heating 
and cooling was higher than the case of 75% occupancy.

In Table 18 to 22, the primary energy use for heating and cooling, is presented for the 
different window variations of the five different building modules, due to the use of the glazing 
units with solar control coating, for 50% occupancy. The results are also compared with the 
primary energy use for these two systems when the occupancy was 100%. 
Once again, the results were similar with the results of the variation case of 75% occupancy, 
but due to the higher reduction of the internal gains related to the reduction of the occupants 
from 100% to 50%, the increase in the primary energy use for heating was higher and the 
reduction in the primary energy use for cooling was lower than the reduction of the occupants 
from 100% to 75%.
