8.5. Sensitivity analysis based on the minimum ventilation rate during the unoccupied 
schedule.  
In the investigation process so far, the ventilation system was operating based on the DCV 
approach between a minimum and a maximum ventilation rates. The maximum ventilation rates 
were calculated according to the equation 6 and based on the number of occupants which it was 
assumed only for the calculation of the ventilation rates in Table 4.6.2 of Chapter 4: Reference 
building system, for each zone of the five different building modules. However, the minimum 
ventilation rate it was selected to be the minimum ventilation rate related to the building 
emissions of 0.7 l/sm2, as it was defined in EN 15251 [5]. The ventilation system was operating 
all the hours of the year, providing only the minimum ventilation rate during the unoccupied 
hours. The selection of this minimum ventilation rate was quite effective regarding the dilution 
of the CO2 concentration (ppm) to the ambient levels during the unoccupied hours. However, 
the primary energy use for ventilation was quite increased. 
In this sensitivity analysis, the operation of the ventilation system during the unoccupied 
hours was changed. According to EN 15251, in the section B.4 [5], it was suggested that the 
ventilation system should be either start to operate two hours before the beginning of the 
occupancy schedule, providing outdoor air of 2 ach, or instead of a prestart, the ventilation 
system could be operating during the unoccupied hours by providing a minimum ventilation 
rate of either 0.1 l/sm2 or 0.2 l/sm2, which was quite lower than the 0.7 l/sm2 which was initially 
suggested according to equation 6. In this sensitivity analysis, it was selected to reduce the 
minimum ventilation rate during the unoccupied hours from 0.7 l/sm2 to 0.1 l/sm2. However, in 
IDA ICE it was possible to define only one minimum ventilation rate for the operation of the 
VAV system based on the CO2 concentration level. As a result, the minimum ventilation rate 
of 0.1 l/sm2 was not possible to be changed into 0.7 l/sm2 during the occupied hours. This 
limitation could differentiate the operation of the ventilation system in the IDA ICE modules 
comparing to the real conditions. 
The number of occupants was not changed and the designed 100% occupancy level was 
used in tis sensitivity analysis. The rest of the designed parameters were used for the evaluation 
of the five building modules in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate, were also 
remained unchanged. The calculation of the primary energy use in the different variation of the 
five building modules was once again performed through dynamic simulations in IDA ICE 
based on the same conditions as they were described in Chapter 4: Reference building system. 
8.6. Primary energy use results for 0.1 l/sm2 minimum ventilation rate during the 
unoccupied hours 
The primary energy use for ventilation in the five different building modules based on the 
DCV strategy and the minimum ventilation rate of 0.1 l/sm2 during the unoccupied hours is 
presented in Table 8.6.1. The results are compared with the cases when the minimum ventilation 
rate was 0.7 l/sm2.The results were the same in all the different window variation cases 
examined because the ventilation system was designed to provide fresh air into the building 
modules and it was not designed for space heating or cooling. 
Table 8.6.1. Primary energy use (kWh/m2) for ventilation due to the DCV strategy with minimum ventilation 
rate of 0.1 l/sm2 during the non-occupied hours and a comparison with the use of 0.7 l/sm2 as the minimum 
ventilation rate, for the five reference building modules.

121 
 
According to the results, even with the design 100% occupancy, the reduction of the 
minimum ventilation rate during the unoccupied schedule resulted into a quite significant 
reduction in the primary energy use for ventilation. Furthermore, as the designed number of 
occupants in the building modules was lower, the reduction in the primary energy use for 
ventilation was higher.  
The reason was that the CO2 production in the building modules of lower number of 
occupants was lower during the occupied schedule. As an example, as it is illustrated in Figure 
8.6.1 to Figure 8.6.4, in the beginning of the occupancy schedule, when the CO2 level was 
below 850 ppm, the minimum ventilation flow of 0.7 l/sm2 was provided in the building module 
of 15 m width for almost one hour in contrast with the building module of 18 m width, when 
the minimum ventilation flow in the beginning of the occupancy schedule was provided for 
almost half an hour. When the minimum ventilation changed to 0.1 l/sm2 in both building 
modules, the minimum ventilation rate of 0.1 l/sm2 was provided for half an hour. As a result, 
the higher period of time when the minimum ventilation flow of 0.7 l/sm2 was provided into 
the building module of 15 m width was the reason for the higher decrease in the primary energy 
use for ventilation. 
 
Figure 8.6.1. CO2 concentration (ppm) (left) and ventilation rates (l/s) (right) in the South workplace 1 of the 
landscape building module of 15 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 0.1 l/sm2 minimum 
ventilation rate, as they were illustrated in IDA ICE. 
 
Figure 8.6.2. CO2 concentration (ppm) (left) and ventilation rates (l/s) (right) in the South workplace 1 of the 
landscape building module of 15 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 0.7 l/sm2 minimum 
ventilation rate, as they were illustrated in IDA ICE.

landscape building module of 18 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 0.1 l/sm2 minimum 
ventilation rate, as they were illustrated in IDA ICE.

Figure 8.6.4. CO2 concentration (ppm) (left) and ventilation rates (l/s) (right) in the South workplace 1 of the 
landscape building module of 18 m width, due to the use of the DCV strategy and for 0.7 l/sm2 minimum 
ventilation rate, as they were illustrated in IDA ICE. 
Between the building modules with the same number of occupants but increased floor area 
the decrease in the primary energy use for ventilation was higher because the workplace per 
occupant was higher and the period of time that the minimum ventilation rate was provided was 
higher.  
Except from the reduction in the primary energy use for ventilation, the reduction of the 
minimum ventilation flow affected also the primary energy use for heating and cooling. The 
lower ventilation rate (l/s) in the unoccupied hours resulted into lower air changes per hours 
(ach) and the age of air (h) inside the building modules was higher. As the indoor air was 
remained inside the building for higher periods of time, the solar gains combined with the 
internal gains resulted into higher mean air temperatures (oC). An example of the mean air 
temperature (oC) of the South workplace 1 in the landscape building module of 10 m width, for 
the window variation case of 2.6 m length, when the minimum ventilation rate was 0.7 l/sm2 
and 0.1 l/sm2 are illustrated in Figure 8.6.5, from 17th to 23th of April. 
 
Figure 8.6.5. Mean air temperature (oC) of the South workplace 1 in the landscape building module of 10 m, 
for the window variation case of 2.6 m length, when the minimum ventilation rate was 0.7 l/sm2 (left) and 0.1 l/sm2 
(right), from 17th to 23th of April. 
In Figure 8.6.6, the age of air (h) for the same zone it is illustrated on 18th of April, when 
the minimum ventilation rate was 0.7 l/sm2 and 0.1 l/sm2. 
 
 
Figure 8.6.6. Age of air (h) of the South workplace 1 in the landscape building module of 10 m, for the window 
variation case of 2.6 m length, when the minimum ventilation rate was 0.7 l/sm2 (left) and 0.1 l/sm2 (right), on 18th 
of April.

Taking into account that the mean air temperature (oC) was higher during most of the year 
period when the minimum ventilation rate was decreased, the energy requirements for heating 
were lower and the energy requirement for cooling were significantly higher.  
In the window variation cases that the solar gains were limited due to the dynamic operation 
of the external blinds, the increase of the primary energy use for cooling was lower comparing 
with the window variation cases when glazing units with solar control coating were used. In 
contrast, the reduction of the primary energy use for heating was higher when the glazing units 
with solar control coating were used. 
In Table 8.6.2 to Table 8.6.6, the primary energy use for heating and cooling and the total 
primary energy use of these two systems, are presented for the different window variations of 
the five different building modules due to the use of the external blinds, and for 0.1 l/sm2 as 
minimum ventilation rate. A comparison with the minimum ventilation rate of 0.7 l/sm2 is 
performed.

In all the five different building modules, the primary energy use for heating and cooling 
was higher after the reduction of the minimum ventilation rate to 0.1 l/sm2. In the building 
modules with higher internal gains related to the higher number of occupants, the increase in 
the primary energy use for heating and cooling was significantly higher, comparing with the 
building modules of lower number of occupants. In the building modules with the same number 
of occupants but different building width, the increase in the primary use for heating and 
cooling was depended on the solar irradiation set point of the external blinds operation. When 
the solar irradiation set point was higher, the increase in the primary energy use for heating and 
cooling was also higher. 
The increase in the primary energy use for heating and cooling in the single man office 
building module of 10 m width, due to the reduction of the minimum ventilation rate, it was 
higher than the landscape building module of the same width. Even if the number of occupants 
was lower in the single man office module than the landscape module, the higher solar 
irradiation set point of the external blinds, resulted into higher solar gains.

According to the results, the reduction of the minimum ventilation rate minimized the 
primary energy use for heating while the primary energy use for cooling was significantly 
increased. In all the different building modules except the landscape building module of 18 m 
width, the increase of the primary energy use for heating was more significant than the 
reduction of the primary energy use for cooling, and as a result the total primary energy of the 
two systems was higher. In the landscape building module of 18 m width, the decrease of the 
primary energy use for heating was higher than the increase of the primary energy use for 
cooling. Due to the higher building width, the effect of the direct solar gains in the more 
centralized areas during the sunny days was lower in contrast with the reduced heating 
requirements during the winter days related to the higher mean air temperature. 
In the building modules of lower number of occupants, the increase in the primary energy 
use for heating and cooling was lower due to the lower internal gains, in comparison with the 
building modules of higher number of occupants. Moreover, as the building width was 
increasing the increase in the primary energy use for heating and cooling was lower due to the 
limited effect of the direct solar gains in the more centralized areas of the wider building 
modules.  
As the glazing area was decreasing, in both the window variations with the use of the 
external blinds and the glazing units with solar control coating, the heat losses through the 
glazing area were lower and as a result the share of the internal gains in the energy balance of 
the building modules was higher. 
8.7. Sensitivity analysis related to the reduction of the Light Transmittance (LT) and the 
solar heat gain coefficient (g) of the glazing units with solar control coating 
In the investigation process so far, three glazing units with solar control coating were used 
in the different window arrangements variations of the five building modules. As it was 
described in Chapter 5: Daylight and lighting, the three glazing units with solar control coating 
were special constructions of the default Futura+i glazing unit by IDEALCOMBI A/S [24], 
constructed by Pilkington glass panes through the use of the online software Spectrum [32], 
also by Pilkington. 
The first glazing unit was the default special glazing unit with solar control coating of the 
Futura+i window products:  4wC(70)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4. The U-value was 0.5 W/m2K, the 
LT was 66% and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 36%. The second glazing unit was 
special enhanced glazing unit with solar control coating of the “Futura+i Fixed light window”: 
6wC(70)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4. The U-value was 0.53 W/m2K, the LT was 66% and the solar 
==PAGE:128==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
128 
 
heat gain coefficient (g) was 36%. The third glazing unit was the special enhanced glazing unit 
with solar control coating of the “Futura+i Fixed light window” and the 4-pane special glazing 
unit of the Futura+i window: 4wS(3)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4w-18Ar-S(3)4w. The U-value was 
0.35 W/m2K, the LT was 61% and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 34%. In all the three-
different glazing units the outer glass pane was the Pilkington Suncool 70/35 OW of either 4 
mm or 6 mm thickness. 
According to the results of the primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling, as they 
were presented in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate, the implementation of the 
three glazing units into the different window variation resulted into quite high primary energy 
use for cooling. In addition, there was an unbalance between the primary energy use for lighting 
which it was significantly lower than the primary energy use for cooling.  
In this part of the sensitivity analysis, the investigation was focused on minimizing the 
primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling, by achieving balance between the primary 
energy use for lighting and cooling. To achieve this balance, the glazing units were adjusted so 
as the LT and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) to be reduced, in order the primary energy use 
for cooling to be decreased and the primary energy use for lighting to be increased.  
In order to optimize the glazing units with solar control coating, the outer pane of Pilkington 
Suncool 70/35 OW, was replaced in Spectrum online software by the Pilkington Suncool 50/25 
OW and it was implemented in the window variations of the landscape building modules of 10 
m, 13 m and 15 m width and the single man office module of 10 m width. In the window 
variation cases of the landscape building module of 18 m width, the outer glass pane was 
replaced by the Pilkington Suncool 60/31 OW. However, the minimum thickness of the two 
new glass panes used was 6 mm. As a result, one glazing unit was used in all the different 
window variation of the 3-pane glazing units and one glazing units was used in the window 
variations of the 4-pane glazing units. 
The reason for the selection of the different glass pane in the landscape building module of 
18 m width was the higher building width. The influence of the solar gains in the more 
centralized areas of the building was less significant and the primary energy use for lighting 
and cooling was similar, according to the results in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor 
climate. In addition, the minimum Daylight Factor (DF) (%) in the critical workplaces was 
close to the minimum requirement of 2%, according to the Danish Building Regulations of 
2015 [4], based on the results in Chapter 6: Improved façade element. A significant decrease in 
the LT could reduce the minimum DF below the minimum requirement in the critical 
workplaces. 
The 3-pane glazing unit with solar control coating with the Pilkington Suncool 50/25 OW 
outer pane was the: 6wC(50)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4. The U-value was 0.53 W/m2K, the LT was 
47% and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 26%. The 4-pane glazing unit with solar control 
coating with the use of the Pilkington Suncool 50/25 OW was: 6wC(50)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4w-
18Ar-S(3)4w. The U-value was 0.36 W/m2K, the LT was 44% and the solar heat gain 
coefficient (g) was 24%. The 3-pane glazing unit with solar control coating with the use of the 
Pilkington Suncool 60/31 OW was: 6wC(61)-16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4. The U-value was 0.53 
W/m2K, the LT was 56% and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 31%. The 4-pane glazing 
unit with solar control coating with the use of the Pilkington Suncool 60/31 OW was: 6wC(61)-
16Ar-4-18Ar-S(3)4w-18Ar-S(3)4w. The U-value was 0.36 W/m2K, the LT was 52% and the 
solar heat gain coefficient (g) was 28%. The new created glazing units are also presented in 
Appendix B: glazing units. 
A new daylight investigation was performed in order to identify the minimum DF (%) in the 
permanent workplaces of the five building modules after the new glazing units with solar 
control coating were implemented in the different window variation cases. The calculation of 
the primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling was performed through dynamic 
simulations in IDA ICE. The lighting design, the heating and cooling system, the ventilation

Primary energy use and indoor climate.

According to the results, the reduction in the LT of the glazing units resulted into less 
acceptable window variation cases in the five building modules. In the landscape building 
modules of 13 m and 18 m width, with three workplaces close to the façade the reduction in the 
LT of the glazing units almost eliminated the acceptable window variations. more specifically, 
in the landscape building modules of 18 m width, the minimum possible reduction in the LT 
resulted into only two acceptable window variation cases. However, due to the higher building 
width combined with the fact that the non-acceptable cases resulted into 1.9% minimum DF in 
the more centralized workplaces, it was decided to accept all the four window variations. 
In the landscape building modules of 13 m width, the significantly higher primary energy 
use for cooling in comparison with the primary energy use for cooling for the glazing units with

and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) when the new glazing units with solar control coating 
were implemented, the primary energy use for lighting heating and cooling was reduced but the 
balance between the primary energy use for lighting and cooling was not the optimal.

As a result, the implementation of glazing units with solar control coating was optimal in 
the landscape building modules of lower workplaces close to the façade. As the building width 
was decreasing, the LT and the solar heat gain coefficient of the glazing units should be further 
reduced in order to create a balance between the primary energy use for lighting and cooling. 
The reason was that the effect of the solar gains in the building modules of lower width was 
more significant. When the balance between the primary energy use for lighting and cooling 
was achieved the primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling was the minimum 
possible.

8.10. 
Sensitivity analysis related to the position of the building modules in a multi-
storey building. 
In the investigation process so far, the examined five building modules was assumed that 
they were in the middle of a multi-storey office building system. They were examined taking 
into account that the two facades were the only external surfaces of the modules. In real 
conditions, in a multi-storey building system there are more external surfaces than the façade. 
There is the roof in the top of the building, the external floor in the lower level of the building 
in connection with the ground, and there are also external walls except from the facades.  
In this investigation process, it was assumed that the building system was a rectangular 
multi-storey building. As a result, except from the two South and North oriented facades, there 
were two side walls oriented in the East and West direction of the building. 
In this sensitivity analysis, in each of the five building modules for the window arrangement 
variation of 2.6 m length and 4.52 m2 glazing area, for the use of the external blinds, as they 
were investigated in Chapter 7: Primary energy use and indoor climate, the internal ceiling and 
the internal floor elements were replaced by a roof and an external floor element. Dynamic 
simulations through IDA ICE were performed in order to identify the differences in the primary 
energy use for heating and cooling due to the additional transmission heat losses through the 
roof or the external floor. In addition, one of the internal side walls was replaced by an external 
wall element in order to identify the difference in the primary energy use for lighting, heating 
and cooling, due to the reduced reflectance of the inner surface of the side wall and due to the 
additional transmition heat losses through the external side wall area.  
The construction materials of the roof element and the external floor element, along with 
their thermal properties are presented in Table 8.10.1 and Table 8.10.2. The construction 
materials layers are presented from the top surface to the bottom surface of the two elements. 
The properties of the material used were according to the IDA ICE default material library and 
based on the Apache-Tables User guide of Integrated Environmental Solutions (IES) Virtual 
Environment [21].   
For the construction of the side external wall element, the external wall element with U-
value of 0.095 W/m2K and thickness of 0.5 m, that it was created in Chapter 4: Reference 
building system and it was used as the reference façade wall element it was selected. 
The lighting design, the heating and cooling system, the ventilation system, the number of 
occupants and the climate conditions were identical to Chapter 7: Primary energy use and 
indoor climate.

8.11. 
Primary energy use for lighting, heating and cooling, due to the different 
position of the five building modules in the multi-storey office building

According to the results, the primary energy use for lighting was increased only when the 
side internal mirror wall was replaced by a real external side wall of lower light reflectance. 
The increase it was close to 32% in the landscape building modules without meeting room in 
the center while the increase in the building modules with meeting room in the center was close 
to 16%. The reason for this difference was the presence of the glazing partition walls between 
the meeting room which they were reduced the effect of the daylight in the meeting room. in 
the single man building module the reflectance of the inner surface of the side partition walls 
was remained the same and as a result there was no difference in the primary energy use for 
lighting. 
The primary energy use for heating in all the different variations was increased due to the 
higher transmition heat losses from the additional external surfaces. For the same building 
modules, the increase of the primary energy use for heating was lower in the case of the 
additional transmition heat losses through the floor, due to the constant ground temperature of 
10oC during the year period. Also, the increase in the primary energy use for heating due to the 
transition heat losses from the external side wall was slightly higher than through the roof 
because the area (m2) of both of them was equal, but the U-value (W/m2K) of the roof element 
was slightly lower than the external wall. Regarding the different building modules, as the 
number of occupants was increasing, the increase in the primary energy use for heating due to 
the additional transition heat losses was higher. The reason was that the operative temperature 
in the building modules of higher internal gains was higher during the year and the temperature 
difference between the external surfaces was higher. As a result, the transmition losses were 
higher, and the energy requirements for heating also higher. In the building modules of the 
same number of occupants but higher building width, the increase in the primary energy use 
for heating was slightly higher, because the area of the external surfaces was higher. As a result, 
the increased area of the external elements resulted into higher transmition heat losses and 
higher energy requirements for heating. 
The primary energy use for cooling was decreased in almost every variation examined, due 
to the heat removed as transmition heat losses through the increased area of the external 
surfaces. The differences in the same building module were again related to the different U-
values of the roof and external wall elements and the constant ground temperature in the case 
of the external floor. In the building modules of higher number of occupant, the reduction of 
the primary energy use for cooling was higher also due to the increased transmition heat losses, 
as it was explained previously. Also, the increase of the external surfaces as the building width 
==PAGE:136==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
136 
 
was increasing and the number of occupants was remained constant, resulted into a further 
reduction of the cooling requirements. 
 
 
==PAGE:137==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
137 
 
Chapter 9: Design recommendations 
9.1. Introduction 
In the investigation procedure so far, different parameters regarding the design of an office 
building system were investigated and their effect on the primary energy use and indoor climate 
was identified. Although, the effect of the different investigated parameters and the different 
design solutions into the office building system was not the same. 
As a result, in this Chapter, the design of the office building system is optimizing based on 
design recommendations. These recommendations were based on the minimum results 
regarding the primary energy use and the indoor climate for the different design parameters 
were investigated so far. 
However, due to the majority of the different parameters were investigated, the design 
recommendations are proposed based on different boundary conditions. These boundary 
conditions are the different specific requirements in order to provide flexibility into the different 
clients. 
9.2. General recommendations 
According to the results of the investigation process, the use of the new implemented 
lighting control strategy based on the dimming operation of the lighting system minimized the 
primary energy use for lighting in the different building modules. Furthermore, if the 
illumination set point could be reduced from 500 lx to 300 lx in the workplaces and meeting 
rooms the primary energy use for lighting would be further reduced. The improved façade 
spandrel panels of 0.175 m thickness and U-value of 0.138 W/m2K was the optimal selection 
for the design of the façade elements. According to the results in Chapter 7: Primary energy use 
and indoor climate and Chapter 8: Sensitivity analysis, the use of the minimum ventilation rate 
of 0.1 l/sm2 and the lower possible occupancy level, significantly reduced the overall primary 
energy use in the different building modules. 
According to Table 8.6.2 to Table 8.6.11 of Chapter 8: Sensitivity analysis, it was concluded 
that the primary energy use for cooling was the dominant share of the overall primary energy 
use in most of the different window variations in the five building modules. The use of the 
external blinds resulted in lower values for the overall primary energy use, comparing to the 
use of glazing units with solar control coating. The reason was that the external blinds reduced 
the LT and the solar heat gain coefficient (g) of the window elements to 6.6% and 7.4% when 
they were drawn, comparing to the constant LT of 66% and solar heat gain coefficient (g) of 
36% of the glazing units with solar control coating. On the other hand, the use of glazing units 
with solar control coating resulted into lower primary energy use for lighting and heating but 
the primary energy use for cooling was significantly higher. 
As a result, for the specific cooling system used based on an electric chiller with Coefficient 
of Performance (COP) of 3, the optimal solution was the use of the external blinds as a passive 
cooling strategy due to the lower primary energy use for cooling.  
However, if there was an option to provide a share or the total energy requirement for 
cooling as free cooling, based on Renewable Energy Sources (RES) or natural venting, in both 
cases, the dominant share of the overall primary energy use would not be the primary energy 
use for cooling. If the primary energy use for cooling was not taken into account, the optimal 
solution would be the use of the glazing units with solar control coating due to the lower primary 
energy use for lighting and heating comparing to the use of external blinds. 
In addition, except the results regarding the overall primary energy use, the implementation 
of a glazing units with solar control coating would be a cost effective and robust solution than 
the use of the glazing units without solar control coating, combined with the implementation of 
the external blinds with all the relevant controllers and sensors. More specifically, the initial 
cost would be similar for the implementation of the window elements in both cases, but the 
initial cost of the implementation of the external blinds would be eliminated. Probably the initial 
==PAGE:138==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
138 
 
cost of the glazing units with solar control coating would be slightly higher than the glazing 
units without solar control coating, but the elimination of the extra cost for the external blinds 
would reduce the total initial cost for the use of the glazing units with solar control coating. 
Furthermore, the operation cost in the case of the glazing units with solar control coating would 
be negligible comparing to the operation cost for the dynamic external blinds. 
The use of the glazing units with solar control coating of lower LT up to 47% and a reduced 
solar heat gain coefficient of (g) up to 26%, were the optimal solution only for the occupancy 
level of 100%. The reason was that the minimum acceptable window variation case was of 
higher glazing area (m2) and the primary energy use (kWh/m2) for lighting was higher in all the 
different acceptable window variation cases of the five building modules. However, when the 
occupancy level was reduced the primary energy use for cooling was reduced also, resulting 
into higher values regarding the primary energy use for lighting and heating in the lower 
occupancy levels, than the glazing units with solar control coating of LT 66% and solar heat 
gain coefficient (g) of 36%.  
When the external blinds were used or the glazing units with solar control coating based on 
a free cooling were used, the building modules with two workplaces close to the facades 
resulted into lower overall primary energy use comparing to the building modules of three 
workplaces for the same window variation cases. In the case of two workplaces, the primary 
energy use for lighting was lower due to the lower building width, the primary energy use for 
heating was significantly higher due to the lower internal gains, but the primary energy use for 
cooling (in the case of external blinds) and ventilation was lower, comparing to the cases of 
three workplaces. However, the building modules of lower number of workplaces, resulted into 
lower acceptable glazing areas, and as a result, the minimum overall primary energy use was 
quite lower than the building modules with three workplaces. 
When the meeting room was implemented in the building modules of the same number of 
workplaces, the overall primary energy use was reduced either for the use of external blinds or 
the use of glazing units with solar control coating. In the wider building module, the primary 
energy use for lighting was higher due to the increased building width, the primary energy use 
for heating was lower due to the same energy requirements for heating divided with an 
increased floor area, and the primary energy use for ventilation was lower due to the minimum 
ventilation requirements of the unoccupied meeting room. In the case of the electric cooling 
system, the wider building resulted into lower primary energy use for cooling due to the lower 
effect of the direct solar gains into the more centralized areas of the building. 
Comparing the single man office building module with the landscape building module of 
the same width, in the case of electric cooling, the use of the external blinds resulted into lower 
overall primary energy use for the single man office module. Due to the higher solar irradiation 
set point for the operation of the external blinds, the primary energy use for lighting was slightly 
lower in the single man office module and the primary energy use for heating was not as higher 
as it was expected due to the lower internal gains related to the only one occupant in the office 
room. However, the primary energy use for ventilation was significantly lower. Furthermore, 
in the case of free cooling, when the glazing units with solar control coating were the optimal 
solution, once again the single man office module resulted into lower overall primary energy 
use. In that case, in the single man office module, the primary energy use for lighting and 
heating was higher, but the primary energy use for ventilation was almost half than the 
landscape building module.  
In the following sections, specific design solutions were suggested for the electric cooling 
system used based on the electric chiller with Coefficient of Performance (COP) of 3.

Conclusion 
In this thesis, an investigation procedure was performed in order to develop an office 
building system, based on the integrated design approach of space of solutions and the 
requirements of nZEB, as they are defined in the Building class of 2020 in the 2015 Danish 
Building Regulations and the EN 15251 standards.  
Several design parameters were investigated regarding their influence in fulfilling the design 
requirements, in order to provide flexibility regarding the needs of different clients. The 
important design parameters were investigated were the different building width, the glazing 
area on the window elements on the façade, the number of workplaces close to the façade and 
the use of supplementary passive cooling solutions of external blinds or glazing units with solar 
control coating. 
The use of a lighting control strategy based on the diming operation of the lighting system 
combined with the reduction of the thickness of the façade wall element, in order to take 
advantage of the available daylight, minimized the primary energy use for lighting. The 
possibility of reducing the illuminance set point from 500 lx to 300 lx would further reduce the 
primary energy use for lighting. As the building width was increasing the primary energy use 
for lighting was increasing. In addition, the use of the Demand Control Ventilation principles 
through a VAV operation of the ventilation system, based on CO2 concentration, minimized 
the primary energy use for ventilation.   
However, in all the building modules of different width and glazing area examined, the 
dominant share of the overall primary energy use was the primary energy use for cooling. The 
primary energy use for cooling was influenced mainly by three parameters: the use of passive 
cooling system of either external blinds or glazing units with solar control coating, the number 
of workplaces close to each façade in relation to the minimum acceptable glazing area and the 
designed level of occupancy. 
In the investigation process, an electric based cooling system was used. According to the 
results, the use of the external blinds minimized the primary energy use for cooling. However, 
the use of glazing units with solar control coating resulted into significantly higher primary 
energy use for cooling but lower primary energy use for lighting and heating comparing to the 
use of external blinds. As a result, if a share or the total energy requirements for cooling could 
be provided through the use of RES or natural venting, without extra cost, the optimal solution 
would be the use of glazing units with solar control coating. In addition, the use of glazing units 
with solar control coating is a more cost efficient and robust solution than the use of glazing 
units without solar control coating with external blinds as shading system, taking into account 
the initial and the operation cost of the two solutions. 
The number of the permanent workplaces close to the facades significantly affected the 
primary energy use in the different building modules. Due to the higher results regarding the 
minimum DF in the critical workplace, comparing with the cases of three workplaces, the 
glazing area could be further reduced without compromising the minimum requirement of 2% 
for the minimum DF. The lower glazing area of the window elements reduced the transmition 
heat losses and the solar gains through the windows, resulting into lower primary energy use 
for heating and cooling. In addition, the primary energy use for ventilation was lower due to 
the lower CO2 production from the occupants. The use of the unoccupied meeting rooms 
increased the primary energy use for lighting but the primary energy use for heating, cooling 
and ventilation was reduced comparing with the building modules of the same workplaces 
without meeting room. 
The design occupancy level, was a major parameter regarding the overall primary energy 
use. As the occupancy level was reduced, the primary energy use for cooling and ventilation 
was reduced while the primary energy use for heating was increased. 
Concerning the indoor climate conditions, in all the different cases examined there were no 
operative hours below or higher than the heating and cooling set points used due to the use of 
==PAGE:144==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
144 
 
the PI controllers and the continues operation of the heating and cooling system in both 
occupied and unoccupied hours. The operative temperature was fluctuating in higher levels 
most of the year period when the glazing units with solar control coating were used and for the 
building modules of three workplaces close to the facades. The indoor air quality expressed as 
the CO2 concentration was always below the maximum acceptable level of 900 ppm, due to the 
use of the DCV system. 
As a result, a further investigation could be performed in order to implement a cooling 
system based on RES, in order to identify the share of the energy requirements for cooling that 
could be provided without cost. Also, a feasibility analysis could be performed for the use of 
the external blinds and the use of glazing units with solar control coating, in order to identify 
the financial benefits of using each of the two solutions. 
 
 
==PAGE:145==
Development and optimization of an office                            M.Sc. Thesis 
Theofanis Benakopoulos 
building system according to the energy  
 
s151007 
requirements in the Danish Building Code in 2020 
145 
 
Further research 
The investigation procedure in order to develop an office building system, based on the 
integrated design approach of space of solutions and the requirements of nZEB, was focused 
on evaluating and fulfilling the energy use requirements and indoor climate conditions as they 
are defined in the Building class of 2020 in the 2015 Danish Building Regulations and the EN 
15251 standards, without taking into account the initial cost or the operative cost of each 
solution related to the different parameters investigated. As a result, it further research should 
be performed to evaluate the optimal design solutions and recommendations with respect to 
their economic efficiency, by performing a feasibility study. 
In addition, the space heating and cooling system in the different modules it was modeled 
by using high efficient ideal heaters and coolers with specific efficiencies. A sensitivity analysis 
should be performed for different efficiencies of the space heating and cooling system, in order 
to identify the relation of the heating and cooling system efficiency to the energy requirements 
and the indoor climate conditions.  
Furthermore, the ideal heaters could be totally replaced by alterative space heating solutions 
such as floor heating or water radiators, due to the significantly low energy requirements for 
heating in most of the different variation cases examined. Regarding the cooling system, an 
alternative solution could be designed and evaluated based on Renewable Energy Sources 
(RES) in order to provide a share or the total energy requirements for cooling without extra 
cost. Also, the influence of natural venting through the window elements in order to reduce the 
energy requirements for cooling could be evaluated. 
Moreover, the use of the 4-pane glazing units could be extended in all the different window 
variation cases examined and an investigation procedure could be performed in order to relate 
the glazing properties of the 4-pane glazing units into lower glazing areas. In addition, the cost 
of implementing the 4-pane glazing elements in contrast with the 3-pane elements could be 
evaluated in relation with the primary energy use. 
Finally, a sensitivity analysis could be performed regarding the properties of the external 
blinds in relation to the primary energy use in the different variation cases examined, taking 
into account the cost of the different solutions.
